LITERARY NOTES .
It is disappointing that Ethel Turner’s new
book of short stories should be disappoint-
ing ; yet it is. One turns back to “ Seven
Little Australians ” to see if the praised
charm still holds ; and it does. Then
why. . . ? Certainly “The Little Duchess ”
and another story or so first appeared in The
Bulletin, and The Bulletin was adorned
by their appearance. Then why . . ?
Perhaps it is on account of the massing :
you must take Little Duchesses in little
doseses : too many sweets cloy. Or perhaps
it is on account of the immaturity : were
not many of these tales composed before
the child-romances ? Or perhaps and
in this there really may be something—
it is because Ethel Turner paints best the
children whom she has known and studied,
not the grown-ups who are nearly all what
her fancy paints them—and nothing more.
And that’s what the public likes, generally
speaking. It’s a nice point to consider
whether they are better or worse off, on the
whole. Does the man of culture who smiles
now and again at Pinero enjoy life as much
as the man of no-culture roars in
ecstasy through a tawdry “variety enter-
tainment”? Were it not wiser to remain
intellectual children, with minds sensitive to
the least air of pleasure-giving excitement,
than to narrow in knowledge the circle of the
emotions, and find all things barren or mono-
tonous. Remember the delirium of Mayne
Reid, the first meeting with W. S. Hay
ward ! Thackeray and Meredith are better,
we know; but are they as good ? Is the
decrease in quantity of pleasant sensations
which comes with age and knowledge com-
pensated by increase in quality ? It’s a nice
point to consider.
* * * *
Only a little aid comes with subtle rele-
vancy from Matthew Arnold :
When I shall be divorced, some ten years hence,
From this poor present self which I am now ;
When youth has done its tedious vain expense
Of passions that for ever ebb and flow ;
Shall I not joy youth’s heats are left behind,
And breathe more happy in an even clime ?
Ah, no, for then I shall begin to find
A thousand virtues in this hated time 1
Then I shall wish its agitations back,
And all its thwarting currents of desire ;
Then I shall praise the heat which then I lack,
And call this hurrying fever, generous fire ;
And sigh that only one thing has been lent
To youth and age in common—disoontent.
• * * *
Yet—resuming—if the public wants emo-
tional cagmag, it might as well have its
cagmag neatly sauced and served. Mr.
Hennessey talks good English, anyway ;
he doesn’t outrage probabilities more than
he must; and he maintains an unusual level
of interest. And, as he is a very industrious
writer, his books are “catching on,” and
verily he hath his reward.
* * * *
In cash, of course, which is far more filling
than critics’ kudos. Becke and Jeffery know
this, who strike the public iron while it’s hot
with ‘ ‘ The Mystery of the Laughlin Islands ”
—a dull booklet which one is sorry to see
Australian writers’ names to. The fourth
and final part of it might have made a short
story—it is all the yam there is : the pre-
liminary padding merely supplies a back-
ground to semi-maudlin characters who damn
each other unconvincingly. The mystery is
a convicts’ escape from Port Jackson, and a
subsequent half-caste population on a South
Sea island—which doesn’t seem so very un-
natural after all.
* * * m
As a fact Becke and Jeffery—the latter’s
name, by the way, is still suppressed in
Publisher Unwin’s circulars—are going
quite the wrong way to work. A little
statement has been blown about to the
effect that “A First Fleet Family” was
written in twenty-eight days, “ only twenty-
eight days ” —as if it were a boast. A boast!
It is akin to that of the local literary lady
who foregathered at a theatre with a careful
and capable dramatic critic, and was as-
tonished when the latter excused himself
towards the close of the performance with—
“ I must go now ; I have a good half-column
to write ; I don’t suppose I’ll get away before
two or three in the morning.” “ What ! ”
said the lady, bristling notes of ex-
clamation, “ why ! I write a column an
hour ! ” She did ; and such a magnificent
column it was. For skipping.
* * • •
There is no getting away from the truism :
easy writing is damned hard reading. The book
written in twenty-eight days is forgotten in
twenty-eight days : the book that lives is the
book that costs a life and takes a life-time.
We want an Australian author to spend, like
Flaubert, years in meditation before spending
years in execution ; the local writer, so far,
is too hasty, too scrappy ; he will not take a
large canvas and ample time—or a small
canvas and ample time —ans create a master-
piece. One seems to see here and there the
minds to do memorable things ; but where
are the zeal, the persistence, the capacity to
“ toil terribly ”to a far-off goal ? Is there
in our febrile national temperament, with its
quick alternations of exaltation and depres-
sion, a something antagonistic to sustained
efibru on any line ? Is it our nature always
to flare fiercely for a moment, never to cast
a steady radiance over an eternity of time ?
* * * *
[And here, half-way up the hill of argu-
ment, a pause is made for the tired reader
to glance at the scenery in a verse or two
from Arthur Symons’s recently-published
“ London Nights” :
AT SEVENTEEN.
You were a child, and liked me yesterday.
To-day you are a woman, and perhaps
Those softer eyes betoken the sweet lapse
Of liking into loving—who shall say?
Only I know that there can be for us
No liking more, nor any kisses now,
But they shall wake sweet shame upon your
brow
Sweetly, or in a rose calamitous.
Trembling upon the verge of some new dawn
Yon stand, as if awakened out of sleep,
And it is I who cried to you “ Arise ! ”
I who would fain call back the child that’s gone,
And what you lost for me would have you keep,
Fearing to meet the woman of your eyes.
A PRAYER TO SAINT ANTHONY OF
PADUA.
Saint Anthony of Padua, whom I bear
In effigy about me, hear my prayer:
Kind saint who findest what is lost, I pray,
Bring back her heart: I lost it yesterday.
SLEEP.
As if tired out with kisses,
Content to be at rest,
Here, on my breast,
Her mouth, that aohed with kisses,
Drooped to my shoulder, then she sighed
A little, smiled,
Then, like a happy ohild,
She fell asleep upon my breast.
Love comes and goes, and this is
(Love, that I once caressed 1)
Love, like the rest,
And goes the way of kisses.
Yet one hour lives, of all those hours that died,
When, like a child,
She turned to me, just smiled,
And fell asleep upon my breast.
Rested ? Allons 1]
• • * •
These be high and solemn questions.
Possibly things will better as the wealthy,
cultured, leisured class increases. (Yet
Buckle’s showing that the progress of know-
ledge depends on the increase of wealth—
for the race—is strangely confuted by in-
dividual examples. Indeed, it is true only
to the point of a specialisation of industry
which need not imply wealth-accumulation
in the ordinary sense of the words).
Hero is a down-at-heel, out-at-elbows author
(neither of these phrases apply locally, by
the way: our facilis-descensus frequently
begins with the hat, or without the shirt—
and as to the ladies ?) who is seized with a
great epic idea—a stage for goddesses and
giants to strive upon. What does he do with
it ?—sit down for five years and make it im-
mortal? No fear; he sits down for five
hours —two, more likely—and turns it into a
short story which may be a minute’s marvel
ere it is swept away and forgotten. But he
collects—poor fellow ! —his guinea.
* * m *
If he would only sit down for a week, it
would be something ; but he cannot afford
even to do that. Yet there are in Australia
three or five fortunate gentlemen who are
able to spend £lOOO a year in buying books
which they never read and rarely permit
others to read. If The Bulletin were as
well off, its thousands and its libraries should
be devoted as that noble bookman Stenhouse
(the so-good friend to Kendall, and Deniehy,
and many a bright spirit else) fifty years ago
devoted his— to encouraging struggling young
writers to shun the snares of superficiality
and give to their generation and their country
the best that was in them. When one can-
not be Horace, how fine to be Augustus !
* * * *
Thus could the rich man in his life-time
do something to kill that pest of the age —
the plague of little books, dull books, weak
books, books more ephemeral than the
ephemeris, yet each doing its possible to
tempt and trap the unwary reader to his
mental undoing. Even all-quarantining
Australia does not quarantine these. One
pardons, of course, a little book to a be-
ginner, especially when it is good; but good
is not enough. We want better, and best,
and better than best. Several bright Aus-
tralian writers are now bringing home credit-
able firstfruits. But .... compare them with
the masterpieces of literature and note the
difference, the distance. Wherein lies it?
That you might toil for a year, through a
volume, to explain— so hard is it to get a clear
mental image of the felt idea, and to put that
image into words which do not utterly cari-
cature it. But to say that those Australians
are comparatively lacking in force of thought
and intensity of expression ; that they want
Passion and Conscience ; that their often
brilliant objective visualisations are pre-
sented as detached impressions, floating out-
side themselves, not brought home and fused
and ordered in subjective consciousness, and
then re-presented in their relation and pro-
portion to the great tin sof existence ,
this is to say something of what should be
said. We seem to want to think and
generalise more ; our logical processes stop
half-way ; we are all induction and no de-
duction ; our details bear no reference to
principles.
* * * *
Of course, one doesn’t desire to turn an
emu into an ostrich : one doesn’t expect that
little Joey will jump as far as old-man kan-
garoo. (This for the benefit of silly people
who won’t read with sympathy, and won’t
understand that any given word has ten
thousand given shades of meaning to a thou-
sand different people, and won’t be content
to take the spirit when the letter is impos-
sible.) If lovely Thais writes beside you,
you take the good the .gods provide you, and
are properly grateful. But, all the same,
The Bulletin will never rest contented till
Australian authors touch the highest stan-
dards set in literature ; and then it will set
the standards higher and preach discontent
anew.
Fancy is a very good thing, but it cannot
take the place of knowledge gained by close
observation. To be strong you must be
natural; to be natural you must study from
nature—paint your landscapes on the spot,
daisy by daisy, as Scott did ; watch
your characters for a week to get the
characteristic trait, as Maupassant did.
The characters in “ The Little Duchess
and Other Stories ” are like the shepherds
and shepherdesses in the china-shop, exqui-
sitely pretty, and dainty, and fragile, and
utterly unlike any shepherds and shep-
herdesses that ever existed. Consider the
story “ Wilkes of Waterloo,” which is beau-
tifully-finished in its way. The author takes
a lot of neat little emotions, and polishes
them with a neat little phrase - duster,
and puts them up on the mantelpiece
each exactly where it ought to be ; and you
feel as you gaze that they are most
elegant ornaments, arranged with charming
taste, and no mantelpiece could look prettier.
And .... after all, why cavil that china
ornaments aren’t alive ? Only because one
hopes for living things from Ethel Turner.
“ Wynnum,” by J. D. Hennessey, of Syd-
ney (Sampson Low), seems a book marvel-
lously well adapted to the public taste. The
public, the British public, the public that
buys—or a good three-fourths of it- -doesn’t
want artistic literature or intellectual litera-
ture. It wants a little romance, and a little
easy humor, and a little blood, and a villain,
and plenty of brave Alonzo and fair Imogene,
and a good bang-clatter of incident all the
way to the happy ending—typical melodrama,
in fact. Ask the experienced newspapers
which print serials. They have found out
that it’s no use feeding first-rate stuff to
fifth-rate intelligences. So London Times,
and Sydney Daily Telegraph, and all the
long stretch of journals between, stick to
unheard-of authors, or authors to bore you to
idiocy (if you read them), and find it pays.
Nothing so easy, say the wise men of the
evening papers, as writing above your public :
you can’t write below them. What does
Waldo’s clerk-friend tell him in “ African
Farm ” ? “ Ever read a book called the
‘ Black-eyed Creole ? ’ That’s the style for
me—there where the fellow takes the nigger-
girl by the arm, and the other fellow cuts
off. That’s what I like.”

BEFORE YOU
T, A. 3P !
When yon go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and co on, throughout a list as long as
(feorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round —
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

THE PROMISE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They dreamed that life would last till love
Was dead, and knew love could not die.
And then the woman heard a Voice
That called her, called her, silently.
It had no substance in its sound,
It stirred no motion of a breath ;
She dulled her ears, it called aloud :
Her lover heard the voice of Death.
She had been his—mate, lover, wife;
Men called her wilful, but he knew
The marvel of her love for him.
In some shut sanctuary it grew.
Love had been lavish, and had laid
A holy softness in her eyes,
That washed their wilfulness away
And led him into Paradise.
He, only, knew the soul of her:
Slowly he learned it, day by day.
One soul alone can see our soul;
The world must stand a world away.
“ Oh, heart of me, and very life, _
Oh, saint and soul, what spirit sighed ! ”
He felt the clasping of her arms.
“Oh God ! It is not Death ! ” he cried.
“ Oh, soul of me and only saint,
Oh, forehead with the gold above ;
Oh, only mouth my mouth has known,
Oh eyes that taught my eyes to love !
“ Oh feet where I forever kneel,
My only potent place of prayer ;
Who could unlock your life from mine,
My woman with the shining hair !
She held him in her woman arms,
Her tender mouth began to moan.
“ They call me, but I will not go.”
(The Voice had loud and louder grown.)
She cried to Love to saviour her :
“ I served thee as a loyal slave.” _
But Love stood back with folded wings :
His eyes were sad. He could not save.
She held her lover in her arms,
And bent his face against her breast.
“ Some day, some night, I will return,
God shail not stay ” —Death stilled the rest.
His woman with the shining hair
And wilful mouth and wifely eyes,
Was hurried from his pleading hands,
Her hair made light in Paradise.
And now the autumn held the earth
Against her gentle, russet breast :
Red leaves with color carpeted
The dust their corpses scarcely pressed :
And nightly all the west was washed
With tones made of moist air and fire :
The poets in their gardens dreamed
Such loves as dying leaves inspire.
The russet of the dying life
Of nature paled to winter white,
And in the day she did not come;
She did not come through all the night.
He saw the shining head of her,
He heard her love-lips breathe his name.
“ I wait, I wait, I wait,” he moaned,
Lest she might hear. She never came.
Not when the wave of spring light broke,
And flowers found breath, and birds found
wing.
Straining through lambent twilights, he
Heard nothing but the stir of spring.
No glimmer of her gown or hair,
No touch of hand, no light of eyes,
Nor hroken breath, nor burning tear,
Came back from lonely Paradise.
Day after day he saw her die,
And heard again that wild, fond breath :
“ Some day, some night I will return ;
God shall not stay.” Then silent Death.
God shall not stay ! Then who had stayed
Her stealthy steps from spirit-land?
He saw her fighting some vast shape
That held her with a ruthless hand.
“ I know that she will come ! ” he said,
And watched and waited, year by year ;
Not Spring nor Summer heard of her,
Autumn and Winter did not hear.
The birds knew nothing of her now
They had forgot her merry voice ;
The flowers forgot her love of them,
Even the jonquils of her choice.
“ She said she would come back,” he said;
And often, in the midnight hush,
He stretched his arms out to the dark
And dreamed his hands had met her touch,
And even dreamed that she was there —
Her gown was still the snowy same—
And smelt the sweetness of her hair,
And.heard her breath. She never came.
After the waiting of a life,
A Voice—not hers—called silently;
His weary eyes were fired with flame,
He stammered in his ecstasy.
“ I knew that you would come,” he cried ;
“ But, oh, beloved, the long, long years ! . .
But when he knew whose call it was,
The flame was flooded out with tears.
Those who were nearest heard him moan
(The hardest could not help but weep):
“ Why did you promise, soul of mine,„
jL promise that you could not keep!
LOUISE MACK.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 1, 1896.
Price, 6d.
G. REID’S PIECE OF COLD IRON.
According to a return presented to N.S.W. Parliament by Premier Reid, certain Government stores which cost £10,132 in N.S.W., cost, in Victoria, £21,069. “That,” said
G. H. Reid, “is a piece of cold iron for Protectionists to chew.” The figures have since been proved to be erroneous in almost every particular, and the official who is alleged to have drawn
them up has been called on for an explanation.
LITTLE BOY AT MANLY: “Here's your bit of cold iron. I've brought it back red-hot for you to chew. Take a bite."

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 1, 1896.
Local Option All Round.
Within the last few years it is sufficiently
obvibus that the Cold Tea movement has
made material advances in Australasia. In
almost every province the alleged blessing of
Local Option seems to be in sight, or very
nearly so ; therefore Parson Boyce rages
in the press with a wilder, madder note
°f joy than heretofore, and Parson Isitt lifts
up his voice on high, and the Cold Tea party in
general is more or less jubilant. Meanwhile,
in all the provinces, the number of public-
houses is steadily becoming less in proportion
to population ; and the consumption of
liquor per head is apparently decreasing;
and 'either the number of aggravated drunks
is also decreasing, or else the police don’t
arrest them with the same vigor as of old.
Whether the teetotal party will help on this
process of reform by trying to forcibly ac-
celerate it, or will create a violent reaction,
no one can tell for certain until the great
experiment of Prohibition is tried. The
point to be considered in the meantime is
that Local Option is apparently coming all
over Australasia, save in those places where
it has already come ; that Local Option is the
natural forerunner of National Option, under
which system a whole State will vote as one
constituency for or against the abolition of
the drink-traffic within its borders ; and that
National Option, as likely as not, will lead,
some time or other, to straight-out Prohibi-
tion. Where Prohibition will lead to, no one
can tell yet—probably to the re-establishment
of the drink-trade as a State or municipal
monopoly ; but this is not the matter at
present under discussion.
• * * *
The question just now at issue is whether
the Option business, either local or national,
shall be carried to its legitimate conclusion,
or whether it shall go just as far as suits
the anti-liquor party’s convenience, and no
further. The Local Option idea is based on
the simple rule that the majority must
govern. This rule has been adopted, not
because the majority is a great success, but
because there is absolutely nothing else to
fall back upon. The majority may be, and
often is, an illogical ass, but so also quite as
often is the minority, and between the two
the only resource is to let the more numerous
ass carry the day. As civilisation advances
the number of matters which are referred to
the decision of the majority necessarily in-
creases. The commiwiity grows more parti-
cular about the things it sees and hears and
smells, and the necessity for compelling the
minority not to make itself a nuisance to the
taste of the majority keeps growing more
urgent. Therefore the time must needs
come when, if the majority objects to the
sight or smell of liquor, or to the manners
and language of the person who has been
drinking it, it shall claim the right to sup-
press liquor to the best of its ability. The
Bulletin doesn’t deny this right by any
means. What it objects to is that the tee-
total fanatic unduly limits the power of the
majority, and wants to have it narrowed
down so ridiculously that if the Cold Tea
crowd is uppermost it shall have all manner
of authority to dictate what the whisky crowd
shall drink, but a whisky majority shall have
no such power in return. In other words,
the Cold Tea crowd proposes a duel in which
it shall shoot at its antagonist until it hits
him, but he shall not shoot back.
* * * *
A very large section of the Cold Tea party
is rampantly Tory in all matters outside its
own little fad. It believes enthusiastically
in the Referendum as applied to alcohol, and
wholly disbelieves in it as applied to every-
thing else. If the Democratic party in the
Legislatures helps the Cold Tea faction to
get its own Referendum, a great many mem-
bers of that faction will thereafter do their
utmost to prevent the extension of that
Referendum to other purposes. Therefore,
Democratic politicians should sit down hard
and fast on all Local Option measures which
refer to drink only, and demand instead
Local—or, rather, National—Option in every-
thing, and make the method of referring
questions to the people the same in every
case. It is necessary, of course, to surround
the system with restrictions—a certain pro-
portion of the Assembly or a certain
number of electors must demand that a
question be referred before the expense of
taking a poll is justified, else the system
would be run into the ground by every casual
crank, and the polls would be unending, and
the expense incalculable. But if the anti-
liquor party can raise the requisition pre-
scribed by law, it should gets its national
vote, just as the State Bank party, or the
Freetraders, or Protectionists, or Land-
Nationalisers, or Single-taxers, or Socialists,
or bi-metallists, or any other party should
do in similar circumstances. It should be
under exactly the same conditions as to the
number of votes required to make the poll
valid and the majority necessary to alter the
existing state of things. In short, the
teetotal-party should have just the same
Referendum as every other party and no
more.
* * * *
The questions which the Local Optionists
desire to submit to a popular vote are
generally three in number : Shall the number
of licensed houses remain as it is, or be re-
duced, or shall licensed houses be altogether
abolished ? If they win, therefore, they
gain something ; if they don’t win they lose
nothing. Under a fair and logical system it
should be competent for the other party, if
it can raise the necessary requisition for a
poll on its side of the subject, to have a vote
taken on the equally pertinent question :
Shall the number of licensed houses be in-
creased, and, if so, to what extent ? The
Local Optionist demands the power to take a
popular vote as to whether the minority
shall be compelled not to drink whisky, and
it is equally just that the other party, if it
can comply with the terms of the Referen-
dum Act, should be able to take a vote as to
whether the minority shall be compelled
to drink whisky, or at least to pay
the State for the whisky which the
majority thinks, on the basis of its own
experience, it ought to drink. And along
with the popular vote for the abolition of
the whisky-seller there should be exactly
similar facilities for taking a vote as to the
abolition of the teetotal lecturer, in case a
majority of the people should desire to get
rid of him. The fact that there is very little
probability of any one ever obtaining a poll
on the latter question makes no difference
whatever in the equity of the situation.
Every question has two sides, and a scheme
which carefully provides that the people
may, if they like, vote aggressively in
favor of one side, but only defensively
in favor of the other, is wholly ridiculous.
If any Protectionist politician suggested a
popular vote as to whether the New’ South
Wales tariff shall remain as it is, or be
altered in the direction of Protection, but
proposed that there should be no vote at all
on the third possibility—a further advance
towards Freetrade ; or if a Freetrade poli-
tician suggested a popular vote as to whether
the tariff should remain as it is, or be
abolished altogether, but demanded that no
one should be allowed to vote for Protection
by the simple process of leaving it out of the
ballot-paper altogether—the people would
probably set him down as a person who was
too absurd for further consideration. Yet
this is exactly the sort of thing the Local
Optionists demand, arid their absurdity has
apparently not yet fully dawned upon any-
body.
* * * •
The last court of appeal in a democratic
community is the majority. It is not by
any means a perfect court of appeal, but it
is the only one possible. The extent to
which the majority may rightly interfere
with the personal liberty of the minority is
absolutely undefined, and the only power
which can define it is the majority itself. If
the majority says it has the right to decide
that the minority shall not drink alcohol,
then it has the right—the practical right,
anyway —for the last court of appeal has
said so, and the matter ends there until
the court changes its views. If the majority
says that it has the right to make the
minority drink alcohol, then it has that right
also, for the sufficient reason that no one save
the majority can prove, by any effectual pro-
cess, that it hasn’t. It has a right to Local
Option in men as well as in drinks ; the com-
munity which votes the expulsion of liquor
has an equally good title to vote—as was
done under the old Greek law of ostracism—
for the expulsion of an individual whose pre-
sence displeases it. Therefore it has a per-
fect right to hold a Local Option poll to
decide whether Parson Boyce shall or shall
not be sent over the border and warned not
to return under pain of death, on the ground
that he is ugly or foolish or unpicturesque,
or on no ground kt all save that it thinks it
can do without him. And it is no earthly
use for Parson Boyce, while demanding
Local Option in drink, to allege that such
doings as these would be an undue interfer-
ence with the liberty of the subject. The
only practical measure of what is undue in-
terference is the decision of the majority.
The majority— Boyce’s majority—has all due
authority to say that somebody else shall not
drink whisky, if there is enough of it to do
so. And any other majority has an equally
good right to enact that Boyce shall chew
tobacco. The only necessary proof of the
majority’s right in either case is that the
majority enacts that it has the right. There
is no right which the majority hasn’t got if
it enacts in legal form that it has got it.
* * * *
When Prohibition comes to pass, by virtue
of the majority, it will close up the public-
houses, breweries, distilleries, vineyards, and
all other properties belonging to the trade.
It is safe to assume that the owners of these
won’t be compensated, for two reasons.
First, the Prohibitionist majority will prob-
ably decide that they are -wicked people who
deserve no . consideration ; secondly, there
will have to be an enormous increase of
direct taxation to make up for the loss to the
Treasury through the extinction of the drink-
revenue, and another enormous increase for
compensation-purposes would be more than
the country could stand—in fact, the country,
in all probability, couldn’t compensate if it
wanted to. A third reason—that it would
be a cold, hard proceeding to deprive the
minority of its drink against its will, and
then tax it to pay for being deprived of its
drink—would not come into consideration,
for that would only affect the minority, and
the minority doesn’t count. Assuming, there-
fore, as the Cold Tea party alleges, and as
many other people allege, that a Cold Tea
majority has every right to destroy the pro-
perty of drink-sellers and drink-makers with-
out compensation, in pursuance of an object
which the majority alleges to be good, then
a Socialist majority lias an equally good right
to shut up private banks without compensa-
tion, if it wants to start a State Bank ; or to
shut up private insurance-offices in order to
start a State Insurance Department; or to
confiscate the private drink-trade, not in the
Cold Tea interest, but for the purpose of
starting a State Drink Department ; or to
confiscate the church lands, if it had a use
for them; or to confiscate Papson Boyce’s
own tabernacle, if it has a desire to build a
public-house on the site. There is no legal
reason why all these things shouldn’t be
done if the majority enacts that they should
be. There is also, from the point of view of
the majority, no moral reason; the thing that
is right, so far as the majority knows it, is
simply the thing which the majority thinks
is right, and if the majority thinks any or all
of these things are right, then they are right,
for all practical purposes, until it changes its
views. When it changes its views, then they
are not right.
* * * *
The moral of all this is that The Bulletin
believes the Cold Tea party should have its
Referendum so long as every other party has
also a Referendum on exactly the same con-
ditions, hut if the Cold Tea party is against
the Referendum in general then it should get
no more consideration than it gives. The Bul-
letin believes that the dispossessed publican,
brewer, and vigneron should have compensa-
tion for the destruction of their businesses by
law, provided that the manufacturer whose
trade is ruined by the establishment of Free-
trade, or the importer whose business is
smothered by a reversion to Protection, or
anybody eke whose industry is killed ’by
a vote of the majority, is compensated also.
And, if the drink-seller does not have com-
pensation, it doesn’t see why the bank, or
insurance co., or pawnbroker should have it
either, if the majority decides that the State
should take over these businesses. It
believes that the majority has sufficient right
to confiscate the brewery business just as it
has to confiscate any teetotaller’s house and
land to make way for a new railway ; and it
has just as good a claim to enact that the
minority shall not drink as it has to enact
that the minority (Boyce’s minority) shall
be intoxicated every Saturday night, if it
prefers to vote that way. In short, this
paper believes that the "teetotaller should
get just the same Local Option and the same
compensation or confiscation as he gives to
everybody else, and that if he won’t .five
them then he shouldn’t get them. And it
respectfully submits this view of the case to
the last court of appeal—the Majority.
The Burns Myth.
A blasphemous person, himself no mean poet,
sends the following to The Bulletin under the
outrageous heading above printed :
It is true that even Carlyle, who professed
to care nothing for any poetry that was not
epic, turned on his full orchestra in praise of
Burns. But then Carlyle was also a Scotch-
man ; and blood, especially Scotch blood, is
thicker than water. Robert Burns was cer-
tainly a poet, but by no means a great poet.
The light that never was on sea or land ivas to
him at times visible, but the vision was never
long sustained. His was not
the amule pinion
That the Theban eagles bear,
Sailing with supreme dominion
Through the azure fields of air.
How, then, was his world-wide reputation
obtained ? In the first place, through his hav-
ing—at a particularly barren period in British
literature—dared to compose verses whilefollow-
ing the ploughtail. The Scottish reading public
of that day—a very small minority of the popu-
lation—-accustomed to the academic verses ol
their Beatties and Blairs, were paralysed
with astonishment when they heard an authentic
human voice singing lyrically in the fields ol
Ayr. It was to them as though the shovel-
headed mole had become vocal. And so they
regarded Burns as a sort of incarnated Scotch
miracle. They, in their learned ignorance,
looked upon him as an example of daemonic in-
spiration because he had (what they had not]
the eyes to see the beauty and the poetry ol
“common things.” As though a ploughman,
starting at the beginning of his furrow at dawn,
and having the sky above him and the air
around him all through the day, had not (if he
had the singing-gift) a better schooling in the
Poetic Academy of Nature than the cultured
gentlemen who wrote about the dawn by lamp-
light ! As a matter of fact, the odds were all
on the side of Burns. But none of his innu-
merable critics and laudators appear to have
had even a dim suspicion of this fact. Another
reason why Robert Burns is so idolised by his
compatriots : he was a humorist, and, as such,
a phenomenon in the kingdom north of the
Tweed. To alter the lines of Pope to suit the
occasion—
Humor in Scotland is so very rare
Scots wonder how the devil it got there.
And, therefore, it is not unnatural that they
should combine to worship the Bard of Ayr.
He supplied a national want —or, let me say, a
national deficiency. But supposing that the
songs of Burns were divested of the original
airs to which they were written—“Ye Banks
and Braes o’ Bonny Boon,” for example—would
they stir the hearts of men as they now do ?
Hardly. And, however the Pattersons and
Johnstones and Armstrongs and their ilks
may like to hear it, all Scottish poetry —
wherein is contained the vision and the faculty
divine—is either emphatically and absolutely
Celtic, or Celtic by suggestion. Nearly all the
old airs, to which Burns wrote words, were
Celtic. The Lowland Saxon could never have
composed them. It wasn’t in him to let his
soul go forth into the night as is the n ay of
the Celt. Take for another instance “Robin
Adair.” This is supposed to be a Lowland
Scottish song, and this is how it begins :
What made th’ Assembly shine ?
Robin Adair!
What made the ball so fine ?
Robin Adair!
And so forth, in the same Cockney strain. The
air to which these fifth-rate dance-saloon words
were written is Irish, and, in its own country,
is called “Aileen Aroon.” It is said to have
been improvised by an Irish harper of the 13th
century who was in love with the daughter of
the De Burgos —now called Burke. His horse
was standing in charge of one of his clansmen
outside the castle gate, and he gave the signal of
elopement to his sweetheart by playing upon
his harp and singing in Irish a song, the first
verse of which is feebly and faintly translated
as below :
A hundred thousand welcomes,
Aileen Aroon !
With welcomes more and more
And welcomes still in store
Till love and life be o’er,
Aileen Aroon !
The foregoing, though worlds away from the
true spirit of the original, is, at least, many
times better than the pinchbeck rhymes
associated with “ Robin Adair.” The author of
them was one Carroll O’Daley, and it was his
elopement with the heiress of the De Burgos
which gave to Scott the idea of his ballad,
Young Lochinvar.” If any Scotchman should
feel aggrieved by what has been said up to this
point, the writer is ready to apologise-to him in
the whisky of his country. Furthermore, to
show that he (the writer) has also a soft spot
tor Scotchmen in his heart, he will quote the
iollowing lines from a poem—which ninety-nine
out of one hundred Scotchmen have not seen—
written by an almost-forgotten American poet
named Fitzgreen Halleck concerning th©
Scottish idol :
There have been loftier themes than his,
And longer scrolls and louder lyres,
And lays lit up with poesy’s
Purer and holier fires ;
Yet read the names that know not death,
Few nobler ones than Burns’ are there,
And few have won a greener wreach
Than that which binds his hair.
And still, as on his funeral-day,
C °U earth-couch around
With the mute homage that we pay
To consecrated ground.
Su <* graves as his are pilgrim-shrines—
Shrines for no code or creed confined :
The Delphic vales, the Palestines,
The Meccas of the mind.
But what to us the sculptor’s art,
His funeral-column, wreaths, and urns ?
Wear we not, graven on our heart
The name of Robert Burns ?
Nevertheless, Burns is not a great poet per se
But he is a great Scotch poet. And as a Scotch
humorist he is unique and miraculous—a dis-
tinct and quaint interposition of Providenck.

PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Sabbath Suggestion.
Dear Bulletin,— If there is anything more
urgently wanted than another in Australia just
no w I think it is a solid Anti-Sabbatarian Asso-
ciation. Sunday, as it is kept at present, is a
day of mortification and gloom. lhis more
particularly in Victoria, where the dour Presby-
terian Puritans are both spiritual and temporal
tyrants, and take the best of care that if those
who disagree with them should (by some barely
conceivable kink in the iron chain of predes-
tination) escape having a bad time m the next
world they shall at least—in order to make up
for any mistaken leniency on the part of the
Almighty —have a hell of a time in this one.
Some few days ago Castlemaine Borough
Council, egged on by local clergymen, an-
nounced that for the future it would refuse
permission for the holding of Sunday concerts
in the Botanical Gardens. And the concerts
referred to were, be it remembered, sacred con-
certs. Perhaps this made them all the more
objectionable to the ministers. Nobody likes
to have his monopoly interfered with, lhe
Rev EA. Crawford— may his tribe increase .
-was the only Protestant clergyman who re-
fused to take part in the ignominious business.
Two or three days afterwards Hamilton
Borough Council was applied to by the Blind
Singers, who are now touring the pro-
vinces for a living, for the use of the local
Town Hall on the following Sunday night. Ihe
B.S. guaranteed that none but sacred selections
should be given, but a number of the council ors
rejected the application with pious horror, stat-
ing that the Sabbath should not be desecrated
while they could prevent it. Prom which it
will be seen that if the Lord liasn t sense or
spirit enough to look after His own Imsiness on
what is (entirely without His authority) called
His own Day, there are (in Victoria, at any
rate) plenty of black-muzzled saints to look
after it for Him. Meanwhile, anybody who
isn’t a black-muzzled saint must take the con-
sequences. If he is a citizen of Melbourne he
may spend his Sunday afternoons in any of the
following ways— viz. , by visiting the cemetery,
attending a pleasant Sunday afternoon-service,
lying in bed and sleeping the time through, or,
finally, by getting clandestinely drunk in a
Sunday-trading pothouse. Of these different
methods of murdering time, the pleasant Sun-
day afternoon method is the most dismal.
Some months ago, G. R. Sims, writing on this
very theme, said :
We are all living our lives lor the one and only time.
All the rest of eternity we shall pass somewhere else an
tirohablv amid vastly different surroundings. It is, ifyou
think of it, monstrously unjust that dunng °
snell —at the best, sixty or seventy .veais out of millions
of icons—we should have to submit to the rules and regu-
lations of a handful of narrow-minded, tyrannical bigots,
who object tfl anything that is not intimately connected
with sackcloth and ashes. If we don t ’
we don’t get a little fun and recreation out of our spare
time now, we shan’t have the chance later on. This is oui
chance to get the best out of life and the best out of the
world. If people would only recognise this fact they
would value and prize life and its privileges. and t
would be such a mighty uprising against the sour Killjoys
of Puritanism that they would be swiftly swept away
from the Council Boards of the City and the State, and no
body of bigots would ever again attempt, by absurd re-
striction or monstrous persecution, to reduce the free-boin
Briton to a condition which is half schoolboy and half
slave.
This is just the sort of uprising I am yearning
to see uprise, with bloocl in its eye, here in Aus-
tralia. The strength of our local bigots lies in
their unity and their fanaticism. They made
up t heir minds long ago that the rest of the
community should, in spite of itself, spend its
Sundays in their groanful fashion—or in tribu-
lation of another sort. And, by dogged persist-
ence they have carried their point and brought
the entire public under their dreary yoke. This
n ,i <rht have been thrown off over and over again
if the public could have been shaken out of its
besotted lethargy and made to see what an ass
and slave it was. There is only one way of
doing this, and that is fighting the bigots with
their own weapons, and opposing their Societies
for Keeping the Sabbath Miserable by an Anti-
Sabbatarian Association that will work strenu-
ously and bulldoggedly till it has compelled
the sour-eyed Killjoys to take their shackles off
the Sabbath so that it may once more become a
free and bright and reasonable Sunday.—Yours,
Creeve Roe.
The Medicoes in the Manger.
An Adelaide medico, it is said, has applied lhe
epithet “ cur” to the doctor who comes to take
the vacant hospital headship. This term of
opprobrium probably expresses the professional
view of the case from the narrowest trades-
union standpoint. The origin of the dispute
between Premier Kingston and the physicians
of Adelaide hospital now matters nothing.
The aggrieved doctors had a perfect right to
resign under the circumstances, or under any
circumstances; and they did resign. But now
they exceed their rights Certainly the
spectacle of a union of allegedly cultured pio-
fessional men out on strike has its humorous
side. Their human nature proves to be the
identical human nature met in a union of brick-
layers or shearers. '1 hey abuse the enemy ,
they bribe his customer-patients with free food
and lodging and attendance not to deal with
him ; they write to the trade newspapers
warning comrades that a strike is on, and there
must be no application for billets ; they
denounce as blacklegs the interlopers who per-
sist in coming to take their places, and resolve
to boycott them socially and professional y.
It wants little, apparently, that there should
be picketing, and “ stoushmg, and trouble
with the police, and a call-to-arms of
the local militia. But who is the enemy
of these enraged medicoes in the manger,
who will neither eat Adelaide hospital hay them-
selves nor suffer anyone else to eat it. the
enemy is not Premier Kingston— he suffers
nothing though the hospital should be per-
manently closed. The enemy is the people, the
sick and suffering people, who are to be denied
medical attendance because of a squabble amo g
their officials ! Apparently the Adelaide doc-
tors are quite prepared to abolish the hospital
altogether, and let the sick poor go die, rather
than allow others to take up the humane work
which they scornfully refuse ! fn other words
the medical guild not only refuses to attend the
hospital itself, but will actually not permit any
one else to do so. Which is a fine, large, brutal
attitude for the alleged healing profession to
assume. _
The Municipal Auditor.
Dear Bulletin,— Your remarks re muni-
cipal auditing are very good, lhe Victorian
municipal accounts are also audited by a
Government Auditor who specially qualities
himself for the position by examina-
tion, and receives his appointment from the
Governor in Council. Being, apparently,
backed un by the Government, this qualified
auditor has regarded himself as thoroughly in-
dependent of the men whose accounts he is in-
structed to examine and report upon. it is
proving otherwise. The Local Government Ac
under which the examinations are held and the
appointments made has no 01 J®
auditor to recover his fees. The Council, when
annoyed at the discoveries made by a too-
zealous auditor, simply passes a resolution that
the auditor’s claim is exorbitant and be no
paid. Sometimes a cautious councillor wil
move an amendment that the auditor be called
upon for an explanation. This is invariably
lost. The Council thereby defies the Act. The
Councillors then stigmatise the auditor as a
robber and his fees as legalised robbery, care-
fully omitting any mention of the reve-
lations which have been made. Council-
lors and officials are thicker than thieves
when the latter are incontinently put into
gaol for “retaining” money—the former then
feel they have a grievance against the audi-
tor 1 The next step for the Council to take in
support of its position is to throw dust in the
eyes of the ratepayers by getting inspired para-
graphs inserted in the local press totheeflectthat
the audit took too long—aud the auditors went
into things too far back, and so on ad nauseam.
The only remedy for the auditor is to enter
upon a highly risky and technical legal process
by asking the Supreme Court for a mandamus
to compel the municipality to pay the audit-
fees in accordance with the Act. Seemingly, a
Judge solus doesn’t like the mandamus applica-
and he refers it to the Full Court. Ihe
Judges do not always take things as they come,
so as to when those fees will be received by the
auditor it is a matter of conjecture, lliere are
some half-dozen or so Government auditors now
waiting the decision of the Full Court before
proceeding to take steps to recover their fees, and
it goes without saying the reports of these 'par-
ticular auditors, were all of a highly unpleasant
character. In some cases embezzlements by
officials have been pointed at, and m others the
financial vagaries of the councillors have been
laid bare. The undertaking of contracts by coun-
cillors (of course, illegal) when discovered gener-
ally stirs up the councillors to make nasty
remarks at public meetings upon the auditors,
both Govt, and local. The councillors would
like to say what books and documents should
be looked at and what questions should be
asked by the auditor. The Govt, of Victoria
might fairly answer these questions :
(1) Why was the power to recover fees omitted from the
L< (2) Why was the Minister of Public Works deprived of
his power to decide disputes between cre d it o r S and muni-
cipalities, such power being given him undei the Statute
prior to the consolidation of 1890? „
P (3) Will they remedy a glaring defect in this direction
when they have before them the abolition of the lo^al
aU (4) t Will they have sufficient courage to propose that all
auditors should be qualified by examination
(5) Will they take public opinion as the guide, and detj
the directors in the two Houses by makmg auditors o
companies’ accounts thoroughly independent of the influ
ence of directorates and proxies ?
I write very fully on this subject, because I am
suffering keenly—l have lost my money in the
past through inefficient auditing of the com-
panies where it was invested, and 1 have to
risk my money now to recover audit-fees from
the shire for what I know was efficient
auditing.—Government Municipal Auditor.
Australian Hospital Sweating.
Ed. Bulletin,— Concerning your remarks on
the sweating of nurses by Charity: W hat
about the sweating of medical men attached to
hospitals ’ Just think of the enormous number
of patients treated by the honorary medical
staffs of the hospital! These men are, like
nurses and other servants, subject to the rules
of the Hospital Boards; they perform a huge
amount of arduous professional work and do
not receive any payment whatsoever for these
services ! Sweating does not necessarily in-
volve long hours of work. To make any
servant give his or her services at an
unreasonably low price or gratuitously is
unquestionably sweating. And it does not
matter who is the master your capi-
talist,” the Government, or the Hospital
Board and Charity. Nor does it make any
difference that, as soon as a vacancy occurs on
the doctors’ or nurses’ staff of a hospital, there
are dozens of applications for every post. It is
nevertheless sweating. It might, of course, e
said that the difference between a decent
remuneration for the services and the actual
remuneration constitutes the donation to charity
on the part of the doctors and nurses. But this
is absolutely erroneous, for no doctor would, of
his own free will, spend the money-value of Ins
work as honorary medical officer to a hospital on
charity, because", measured by the ordinary rate
of fees, this money-value of his work would be
at least ten times his total income. It is, there-
fore, not because medical men are more charit-
able than the rest of human society, but simply
because they have allowed themselves to be
sweated that they give such an enormous
amount of workdo hospitals gratuitously, lo
a certain degree, therefore, medical men deserve
their undignified position. Still, the sweating
is unquestionably an injustice, an abuse of
power on the part of the Hospital Boards and
of the community. On the continent of Europe
this injustice has long been removed in a very
simple way. Take Germany : There does not
exist any honorary appointment to a hospital,
no honorary doctor, no honorary board. It has
long been recognised as the duty of the munici-
palities to provide for their sick (and poor).
After one has lived for a certain period in a
municipality this municipality is responsible
for his care and attendance in case of sickness
combined with poverty. The municipalities
therefore own the hospitals and levy from every
citizen a hospital rate just as any other
rate, and from this rate all expenses of the
hospitals medical salaries included are
defrayed. A hospital, therefore, is not an
institution depending upon voluntary contri-
butions of charitably-inclined people, but a
commercial undertaking of the same order asa
municipal gasworks —not conducted for the
purpose of producing revenue, but with a view
to be fully effective on the one part and on the
other to be as small a loss to the municipal
purse as possible. Thus sweating exists just
as much or as little in German hospitals as
in other municipal or Governmental workshops
and offices. And the result is a good one tor
all concerned. 1. The medical man receives
only fair pay for his work, performs Ins duties
cheerfully and with a high sense of duty and
dignitv. 2. The sick, consisting of none but
genuiiiely-poor people, enlist the sympathetic
interest of their medical attendants. S. All
people able to pay for medical attendance are
strictly excluded from the benefits which the
taxpayers wish to have conferred upon the poor
in their midst. Altogether, the system of pro-
fessional men giving their professional services
gratuitously to charity is a rotten one. G.E.
[Then why do they give ? From sheer inherent
altruism? The Bulletin smiles. “ G.L. s sympathetic
statement of the doctors’ case leaves out the cardinal
factor. What is “sweating”? In essence, services
rendered without quid pro quo. And the doctors have no
quid pro quo\ have they not? Then whence the enoi-
inous competition for unpaid hospital appointments bj
medical men in well-paid practice ? For love of humanity
and the greater glory of God ? The Bulletin smiles some
more Why, the quid pro quo is on the surface of the
argument: it is experience; experience which has a
solid cash value for extra-hospital practice. lo
the public hospitals gravitate all the most in-
teresting ” and complicated “ oases —cases which
don’t come to the average practitioner once m
a lifetime. The hospital surgeon makes his _ ex-
periments—gets his hand in; the hospital phj sician
strengthens his powers of diagnosis—fires his drugs in ,
in corpora vili. He cuts and carves, or shoots and hits or
misses, “ without prejudice.” Deaths go to the hospital
account; not to the operator’s. And all the while he is
accumulating most valuable knowledge—knowledge
worth guineas and guineas to him then or afterwards.
He is at least as much student as professor ; he learns as
much as he teaches; and the hospital actually supplies
him with this splendid practice, gives him this priceless
course of study, and throws in subjects, drugs, instru-
ments nurses, operating room, everything necessary to
his success—all absolutely without fee [—“gratuitously,
as “ G.L.” puts it. —Ed B.]
The Privy Council Will-o’-the-Wi&p.
Apart from the Imperialistic device foi tighten-
ing those silken bonds, &c., the chief reason
given for the proposed appointment of an Aus-
tralian Judge to a seat in the Privy Council for
the purpose of colonial appeals, is that in such
cases he would be useful as a sort of expert in
colonial law. Let us see how far his usefulness
in that capacity would extend. Seeing that lie
is needed only as an expert, his presence would
be superfluous in cases which have to be decided
according to the principles of common law only
(and many such go from the colonies to the
Privy Council), becauseour common law issimply
the common law of England, in which the Eng-
lish Judges are, or are supposed to be, more
skilled than ours. The same remark applies
to cases depending on colonial statutes which
are identical with English statutes. So that
we get the sphere of usefulness limited to cases
depending on the statute law of the colonies, so
far as it differs from the English statute law.
Again, as many colonial statutes aie peculiar to
one particular colony, and as there is, generally
speaking, a w'ide difference between the statute
laws of the various colonies, it follows that the
said sphere of usefulness must be further
limited to cases depending either upon statutes
in force in the colony from which the Judge
has been chosen or upon statutes of other
colonies which have a parallel in that colony.
For instance, a Judge from New South Wales
might be useful in a case from Queensland
which depended upon the Real Property Act
of that colony, because there is a somewhat
similar statute in force in New South \\ ales.
But in a case depending, for instance, on the
Dividend Duty Act of Queensland, which is
peculiar to that province (and there is such a
case actually pending now), a Judge from any
of the other provinces would be useless as an
expert, as he would have no more official
knowledge concerning that Act than the
present Judges of the Privy Council.
An analysis of the colonial appeals decided by
the Privy Council during 1895 shows that, if
Mr. Justice Winpeyek, whom it is proposed to
appoint, had been a Judge of the 1 tivy Council
during that year, the very outside limit of his
usefulness as an expert would have been con-
fined to six cases out of 29. The other 23 cases
depended either on purely local laws an
statutes outside of N.S. Wales or on the
common law, or on statutes identical with
statutes in force in England, in all of which, as
has been pointed out, the presence of an Aus-
tralian Judge as an expert is useless and supei
fluous. ;
Ed. Bulletin,— N.S. W. Labor-members would
suffer little financially were the motion to re-
duce their Parliamentary salaries by one-halt
carried. At present they are dogged by
needy followers who practically claim a share
of the spoil. No member who, before entering
Parliament, used to earn £2 or £3 per week can
satisfactorily explain to these followers why he
now needs the whole of <£6 per week. ence,
part he must or prepare himself to be tree ed
as a traitorous enemy. On the other hano, l
the allowance were but <£lso a year, camp-
followers would have no case, and the
member would possess as much as on the
average he is now able to retain. Further, tie
purity of his motives would be less doubted.
This is no small matter. That a man may
Drofit himself and his country at the
same time is quite possible. But the
producer of the twofold profit must stand the
ieers of passers-by. Tories who want to. kil
Labor-representatives could do so by raising
Parliamentary wages to ,£l5 a week. Labor-
representation without payment of members is
on a large scale impossible. Labor-representa-
tion on high salaries, unless the member can be
tied down under legal bond to give most of it
to his class, is equally impossible ; and if so
tied down he diverts the revenue into improper
channels, for, instead of payment of members,
his theory is payment of members and members
hangers-on. —O’B.
Editor Bulletin.— /?e late remarks of yours
as to the intemperance of temperance advo-
cates. How much of the energy of these loud
snorters may be owing to the alcohol they
denounce—energy inherited, perhaps, from
ancestral wine-bibbers? For it can hardly be
a coincidence that the three great progressive
races, the spawning, dominating peoples, the
British, German, and Russian, are now, and
always have been, enormous consumers of
strong drink. Certainly the Briton, since the
time his ancestors drank mead from the skulls
of their enemies, has been a drunkard; Ins
aggressive energy seems to have developed, by
the habit ; for only in this respect is he notably
superior to many of the people he has con-
quered. The south of Europe, Turkey in-
cluded, is notoriously temperate—it should be
the Paradise of Good Templars ; but one fails to
hear of aught exemplary reported from that
direction. Medical testimony, that once sup-
ported the apostolic injunction to “ take a little
wine,” is now nearly solid for the cold-water
party ; but does it not prove too much ? Should
not our race have been exterminated genera-
tions ago, if the cold-water deductions are cor-
rect? Alcohol may be a curse to individuals, it
may cause much of the evil charged against it;
but this is the handicap attached to all special
advantages. You excel at your peril. Nature
gives only two-edged swords. The three-bottle
man, who lords it over millions of Hindoos,
puts his son in an asylum for inherited
alcoholism. Newgate is crammed with criminals,
Westminster Abbey full of heroes, suckled on
the bottle. Genius is notoriously intemperate,
whilst cold-water and mediocrity are twins.
Australia’s future in this respect looks gloomy.
The young bushman has little taste for.alcohol;
he prefers to ruin his digestion with billy tea ;
and to suppose a great race can be developed on
the cat-lap of Confucius is to ignore all his-
torical experience.—H.F.
A Vic. legal correspondent addresses The Bul-
letin ve the Law Society of Victoria, just
established for the purpose of effecting the
unification of the legal profession in Victoria,
and thus to give effect to the Act of the Legisla-
ture passed in 1891 ” :
Some of the reasons that justify the founding of the
Law Society are—
-1 The scales of costs have not been altered since the
professions of barristers and solicitors were combined.
The .Judges have allowed four years to pass without
making any effort to give effect to the new law. Instead
of accepting the new law, they sneer at those who obey
the law ; tney smile upon those who endeavor to make the
law abortive. . ~ __ , ~ , ,
If a lawyer conducts a case in the County Court, he will
be allowed— practically speaking—only one-half the costs
he would have obtained if he briefed another lawyer to
appear at court. Is this right !
If a lawyer makes an application to a Supreme Court
Judge—say in Chambers—he will be allowed one guinea.
If he briefs another lawyer, the Judge will allow 7 three
guineas. Is this right? Thus on.
In spite of the Judges, however, the keenness of compe-
tition in the profession is forcing amalgamation. Already
in the county court, the Insolvent Court, in general
sessions, in the criminal court, in the assize courts
throughout the country, one man conducts the proceed-
ing °Selborne Chambers are agitated. Briefs are fewer.
The future is to the competent man who knows his profes-
sion, not to the society bantling of an attorney who re-
tains another man to give opinions, draw pleadings and
conduct all proceedings.
9 The Law Institute, of course, consists of the swell
solicitors for the banks and building societies, insurance
offices, wealthy squatters, &c., &c. —the old firms accus-
tomed to the old ways. They, of course, don t want to
appear in court. They brief another man to do the work.
Now is it not right that struggling merit should have a
chance ’ Is not open competition the wisest course ? Let
the man who does the work reap the reward. Under the
present scales of costs the man who briefs another to do
the work gets the reward.
3. If the scales of costs were remoulded, litigation
would be much cheaper. . , . ,
4 If the unification of the profession were a real fact
and not a mere theory, procedure would be revolutionised
and consequently cheapened. There would be no expen-
sive briefs to draw, statements of claim, statements of
defence, replies ; seldom orders for interrogatories, orders
for inspection and other interlocutory applications that
prolong proceedings and swell expense. Whilst a law-
suit has to pay (1) an attorney and his clerks (2), a
barrister and his clerk ; but nearly always two and fre-
quently three barristers with their clerks, the costs must
be prohibitive.— Barrister and Solicitor.

dies. The roots are the Poor.” — Zola.
In Sydney E. News Saturday supplement
Mr. G. B. Barton has, for many months past, been
chattily focussing from a thousand sources, manu-
script and printed, sidelights on the early history
of Australia. Mr. Barton has had surpassing
opportunities of studying the subject that ho loves,
and is one of the two men who know more about
it than any others. In his E. News contribu-
tions there is material for a much-needed volume
of the greatest interest to Australians.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The Commercial Banking Co., of Sydney is
almost the only one this half-year which doesn’t show a
downward tendency. 'Jhe profits are £40,303, whereas
the previous half-years panned out for £39,474, £38,037
and £36,599 respectively. The usual div. at 8 per cent,
per annum absorbs £39,965, leaving £338 to the good.
The paid-up capital is now £1,000,000; reserve £1,010,000 ;
and balance to credit of profit and loss, £15,071. The
deposits (£9,976,000), advances, note-issues and cash-
reserve all show a decline, but still the Com-
mercial of Sydney has the magnificent coin re-
serve of £2,388,000; and coin and liquid securities
together amount to £3,066,000. Altogether, it is a very
sohd-lookmg institution, and the fact that its profits are
a little better than they were six months ago is note-
worthy. The last half-year has been one of general
disaster, and almost every other bank in the Eastern pro-
vinces whose June balance-sheet has yet appeared shows
the effect in diminished profits—very seriously diminished
m some cases.

Business is evidently booming with the S.A.
otate Land Bank now that it has really got into operation.
A very few weeks ago it made a start by issuing £20,000
worth of 3J4 per cent, bonds, which were taken up locally
at over £lO2. Since then £lO,OOO worth have been pri-
vately sold ; £30,000 were issued the other day, and were
rushed by the public at a premium ; and another £5O 000
worth are shortly to be issued. This shows that the State
iiank is already doing a considerable business. As it
issues its bonds in small quantities, the Adelaide market
is quite large enough for its purpose, and will probably
continue so, even when the transactions have run into
! n “' I ,°, ns - The bank has no big unproductive balances on
hand to pay interest upon ; the premiums received on the
ponds sold have, so far, gone a long way to pay the work-
ing expenses of the institution ; and the bonds themselves
are a convenient and valuable investment. Altogether,
Kingston s Land Bank scheme seems, so far as the evi-
invpnt h | ave gone ’ the most P erfect thing of its kind
invented up to now.

According to Sydney E. News, two plodding
miners have for two years been mining for gold in the
immediate vicinity of Botany Bay. They have been
working stone of a most extraordinary character, *‘re
tj- 1 ?! , s^es bacon.” Kaolin, similar “to the Broken
liill stuff,” has also been met. They have had to pene
trate the rock with hammer and gad, “explosives being
h O Su- ostly '” Assa^s . show gold ldwt. 15grs., silver l9grs.
This the men admit would not pay to cart for crushing,
but they are confident that, if a small plant could be
erected adjacent to the mine, it could be kept going with
payable results.” It is very important to learn that the
miners living four miles away have to walk that distance
in the morning, and the same distance back in the even-
ing, after working in the shaft all day.

* * * *
At Boggy Camp, Inverell, N.S.W., the other
day, a load and a-half of dirt yielded 724 carats of dia-
monds.
* * * *
The burning question up Nundle way (N.S.W.)
at present is “ Are they diamonds or are they zircons ? ”
If diamonds, then Nundle is going to be second only to
Kimberley, and the Bingara and Boggy Creek diamond-
mines will not be in it, in which case the writer will be
very sorry for Capt. Rogers, the blind diamond octogena-
rian, who supplies the Sydney papers with periodical sen-
sations. The N.S.W. Govt. Geologist, backed up bv Prof.
M Coy, of Melb., has declared that the stones submitted
are zircons, but a London cable says they are diamonds
and worth 10s. a carat, and the people of Nundle on this
occasion prefer to believe the cable. “ Well, if they are
diamonds,” said a miner to the writer last week, “ I know
where I can get a cart-load of ’em ? ” The indefatigable
Dowell “ has pegged out a large area.” This phrase has
appeared so often of late years that the public must
wonder what has become of all the other large areas
pegged out by the same Mr. Dowell, and if they nre all in
an active state of development how much of NSW
mineral lands are left unpegged by Dowell 7

Mt. Gahan co. has started sinking the vertical
shaft to cut the Falkner-Victory lode at 700 ft. No ore
coming out of mine. Shaft will have to be sunk with call
money, and the gold returns from cyanidation of slimes.

* * * *
Falkner G. M. Co. shipped from Eden last week
a large quantity of ore (presumably to blow about at
next half-yearly meeting). It is many months since the
last shipment, and from 16 to 20 men have been con-
stantly employed in getting out this lot.

Some dolt enquires how it is there have not been
more big Westralian crushings after the recent rains,
lact is, in most cases the rain came so suddenly that the
big lodes carrying IOOOoz. to the ton were entirely washed
away, and crushing is therefore delayed while the do ice
make enquiries. *

* * * *
The writer of the articles bn the W. A. goldfields
appearing in Melbourne Age and Sydney D.T. is “ Billy ”
Briggs, a well-known member of the Age staff, who had
some years’ hard practical mining experience on Bendigo,
and was afterwards for some years mining-reporter for
the Bendigo Independent. Unlike 99 per cent, of jour-
nalists who attempt to write on mines, Briggs knows
something about his business. He practically confirms
what has been said in these columns for the last two
years —that the Coolgardie-boom will yet be known as
the biggest fraud of the nineteenth century. Of the 12
miles around Coolgardie, he says : “It is amazing to con-
template the immense amount of capital sunk. It wearies
one to drive mile after mile through this inhospitable
bush and see an endless succession of shafts sunk in the
hope of picking up another Londonderry or Bayley’s
Reward. Some have succeeded in finding fairly strong
bodies of unpayable ore, but a large majority have failed
to even find a reef.”
* * * *
It lias transpired that a parcel, alleged to con-
tain 400 oz. gold, forwarded from a Westralian mine to
Melbourne mint, contained only lOoz. gold, the remainder
being iron. Clever theft or blushless booming—which ?
• * * »
At last another reported rich strike comes from
Bayley’s : “The stone is precisely similar to that from the
mine in its palmy days.” If so, then it’s 1000 to 1 that it
will be lost as suddenly as it was found. As if in its
favor, it is said that the find has not been made in the
Reward mine proper, but at the 100 ft. level from Cock-
shott’s shaft, 60ft. to the north. Other pockets were get
from time to time in the vicinity of that shaft three years
ago.
* * * *
Kalgoorlie : “ Splendid specimens have been
brought in from the Golden Crescent mine, Black Flag,
assaying over 6!)oz. to the ton. It is proposed to float the
mine in the East or on the field.” The writer predicts
that the East will have a chance of going into this show.
A mine with splendid specimens and 60oz. stone could not
possibly be worked without floating. Where would the
money for machinery come from ?

* » * *
The £30,000 representing the unpaid part of the
Plunderderry purchase-money locked up for shortage of
area, was released by order of Justice A’Beckett in Mel-
bourne last week. The owners’ shares, 116,000, have also
been released in London. Mills and party have now had
£lBO,OOO cash and 116,000 shares, and J. Bull has the
mine. But the trouble is not all over. The owners claim
heavy damages at law for the alleged illegal lock'ng-up of
their shares, which prevented them from quitting when
the scrip was as much above par as it is now below.
* * * *
Another crushing from Brown Hill mine, W.A.:
119 tons for 2740 z. This was the mine which the news-
paper hummers declared twice a week for months would
lick the Boulder. It is now all on account of the dry
crushing-mill, which was also going to do great things.
When is the public going to get an official denial that the
bottom of this mine is “ dicky ” ?
* * * *
Great news founded on great assays has been
received and widely published in London about the amaz-
ing richness of the Star of Coolgardie mine, now worked
by the Flagstaff Co. A sample sent to London by the
manager was assayed for a return of 260 z. to the ton.
“This the directors viewed as so exceptional that they
cautioned shareholders against looking upon it otherwise
than as specimens of unusual richness.” A similar
opinion was sent to the manager. However, someone has
officially reported from the field (so it is printed in a
London paper) that “thousands of tons of stone of this
class can he got. If the ore had been free milling there is
no doubt that before now this would have been a notorious
mine ; the only thing to make it so now is a suitable
plant for extracting the precious metal.” The examiner
does not say, it would seem, what sort of a plant would
be suitable to save such rich “ tack.” The trial crushing
put through Bayley’s battery was a failure—from which
it would seem that a stamp mill is quite out of the ques-
tion. Yet the same report says, “ arrangements have
bem made to use 10 heads of the United Mill, and a 50-ton
trial is now being made.” This is dated London, June-18.
At 260 z. the 50 tons should have returned 130)oz. The
Bulletin has not heard of it, but expects to find that it is
all in the tailings, as usual. N.B. —The fact that this
mine, with 200 tons of this 260 z. ore at grass, was sold for
£lOO, does not appear in the London financial papers.

A petrified bone has been found at a depth of
295 ft. in a Creswiek (Vic.) mine, and forwarded to the
Mines Minister. Mr. Foster, says the Age, has placed it
amongst the other fossils of the department. The bone
has the advantage of the other fossils —it has at least been
down a mine.

* * * *
Prince Regent, Ballarat, has had no rich speci-
mens to boost its half’s yield, hence the poor return of
4970 z. 18dwt. from 1765 tons. When the deeper levels are
tackled, however, the mine should do well, if the success
of the adjoining claim is any criterion.
Shenandoah, one of Bendigo’s deep-level div.-
payers, last half knocked 48430 z. lOdwt. from 6953 loads
from the reef at 2782 ft. ; div., £11,200-equal to 7s. per
share. Good quantity of stone yet to stope, and im-
proving as it gets north of the slide met some time back.
New Chum Railway still wears a healthy look,
the reef standing well up to 30ft. thick, and milling a
fair average—about an ounce. Going deeper, another
lift to get at that portion of the reef gone underfoot in the
south end.
Eureka Extended has to face an 800 ft. sink before
it can expect to out the N.C. Railway’s reef—a work that
will bleed scrippers heavily. At present, the co. is
struggling with a thin make of stone at 1230 ft., with
medium results.
Koch’s Pioneer, while seeking the Windmill
Hill reef with its 1250 ft. north level, is stamping divs. out
of masses of spurs opened in the 400 and 1450 ft. levels,
all of the between ground to the north being yet un-
touched. Bi-term results : 5496 loads for 34550 z. lOdwt.—
average 13dwt.; div., £11,875. Good talk for a mine
labelled “ dead ” about a year ago.
New Prince of Wales dropped hack last half to
an Sdwt. average, reducing 2820 loads for 1084 oz. 7dwt.
So far prospecting below 300 ft. has not proved anything
brilliant, but the co. is hopeful that it will shortly happen
on to the rich shoots coming down from the upper levels.
New Moon is the cheapest-worked mine on
Bendigo. Mines and mills for 17s. 6dj a ton: wages,
Bs. 3id. ; materials, timber, wear and tear, 2s. 9Jd. ;
crushing, 4s. 4d.; carting, sid. ; firewood, 6jd. ; pyrites
treatment, B?4d. ; office expenses, 4%d. Takes beating.
The half-year shows the average yield is lower than the
previous term by 3dwt. 13grs. per ton, evidence that the
vast body of flat spurs in the 685 ft. level is badly slump-
ing in gold contents. Divs., £2400.
Catherine Reef, for the fortnight, 288 oz. from
554 tons. Puts the co. £l3OO in credit, so a div. looks
all right next week. By the appearance of the mine serip-
pers are in for a profitable time.
Garden Gully United, once famous for divs.,
has the call-tear in its eye : “To first call, £1630.” Ex-
cepting a small body of payable stone at the 745 ft. level,
the mine is not looking at all cheerful.
New Argus’ past half, 3672 loads for 105i)oz.
12dwt., against the previous period’s 3714 loads for 13220 z.
ldwt. Has paid 55 divs., £45,375, or 30s. 3d. per share.
* * * *
“The Victorian Mount Morgan” is on the
market with reports of its great possibilities. Five tons
put through a battery on Bendigo returned 2oz. Bdwts.,
and on this big calculations have been made. The con-
centrates, of which there were 15 per cent., assayed Boz.
Mr. Thureau, late Tas. Govt, geologist, says 7 dwts. per
ton will cover all charges. E. M. Cairnes,
3dwts. ore should leave a margin for profit even with a
10-head battery. James Clingan, who signs himself
“ mining-manager, Bendigo,” “ full}' believes that this
lode will eclipse Mt. Morgan in size and returns, for the
ore can be mined and crushed for a cost of 3s. per ton.”
These are not the only discrepancies. Thureau says there
is sufficient water-power available to operate 40 to 50
heads; Cairnes, that it is enough for 100 heads, at least,
and Clingan confirms this estimate. The latter is the
discoverer. No mine can be proved a Mt. Morgan by
treating 500 tons, much less five. Why is the report of the
Yic. Govt, geologist, who recently examined the show,
not published?
* * * *
From London comes the first news of the
Frederick the Great crushing (Bendigo)—4so tons, gross
yield 770 z. The manager also wired “disappointed.”
So possibly were the English shareholders. The prospect
of J. Bull and Co. taking any more Bendigo mines after
this is not very bright. Like Australia with English
artists, Bu’l gets Australian mines when they are pretty
well played out.

Thirty tons of gold in all have been won at Wal-

* * * *
Langford, the new boss of Melb. Mutual Store,
got the post by his proved tact and business clever-
ness in driving the Standard Bank through so many
difficulties. Langford retired from the bank manage-
ment to take up the store management at the instance of
Langton, chairman of directors in both concerns.

Tas. mine-returns last month : Tasmania, 324G0z.,
value £12,335; Golden Gate, 1550 tons, 9720 z.—a big
drop from the usual average ; other mines crushed during
the month 4861 tons, for 44000 z. Divs. : Tasmania, £3BOO ;
Golden Gate, £320!); Bischoff, £4500 ; Silver Queen, £2160 ;
Western, £l5OO. In proportion to the number worked,
and all things hang on the law of averages, Tas. has more
big gold, copper, and tin mines than any other part of
Australasia.

The decision of the Judges in the Mt. Lyell
case has caused verv general surprise in mining-circles.
Meanwhile, John Henry keeps the scrip, and Dixon
doesn’t throw shillings about for the crowd to scramb.e
for quite so promiscuous-like as heretofore. The lawyers
smile serenely and buy new winter overcoats.

The number of men known throughout Austra-
lia to be almost total strangers to mines and mining who
attach M.E. and E.M. to their names is one of the most
pitiable things connected with the industry. One signi-
fies Mining Engineer, the other Engineer of Mines.
Neither represents any degree or actual diploma, and the
men who by skill and long experience are so called and
might so call themselves seldom use the terms, so as not
to be confounded with the Crowd who feed and fatten on
misrepresentation, and whose only distinguishing qualifi-
cation is cheek
