S.S.: A capper for your story of the brand-new
miners who used a whole coil of fuse in exploding a
charge. The manager of the Golden Crown had put on a
couple of wild, shaggy “miners,” on the order of a city
director, who guaranteed them to be careful and experi-
enced workers. They went into the winze at No. 2, night
shift, and, at about 2 a.m., the manager was knocked up,
and heard a voice in the darkness: “Be gob, sor ! the
fuse ye gav us won’t do at all, at all.” “ How’s that, you
furious galloot ? ” “ We’ve thried it, sor, an’ it won’t rach
widin fifty fate av the surface.”
* * ♦ *
The latest cry of the Sydney Woman Suffra-
gists : “ Equal work and equal pay, for women as for
men,” is one that the male trades-unionist ought to back
up. Care should be taken, however, that the equal
pay ” does not level down to the present woman s standard,
but that it rises to the man’s. When a typographical
union denied membership to a female compo., it com-
mitted a blunder typical of acts of foolishness that are
being done all along the line of sex demarcation. A
female doctor deserves as high a fee as a male doctor
who is not more efficient than she ; a female typewriter
ought to be as well paid as a male one ; a female compo.
should charge as high rates as her male colleague. The
of females from trades-unions, however, pre-
vents all this. It does not keep the female out of trade
competition ; it merely lowers the “ minimunnwages.”
As a modern mining-camp, Cripple Creek,
Colorado, U.S., wins easily from all others. It is but four
years old, and last year returned one-sixth of the gold-
output of the States. In the absence of fuel, the ore has
to be transported sucb a distance that all under 2oz. rock
is left for future treatment. Of the 2000 claims taken,
over 400 are regularlv sending away ore. Forty-eight
men who went there within the last four years have made
over a million dollars each. The output for the first three
months of this year was 50,000 tons, from which over
£500,000 in gold was got.
Westralia either has the gold or has it not. On
the chance, a mere toss-up, W.A. has been capitalised up
to the capital value of the Rand. The capital has been
mostly got out of British speculators, on reports and pro-
spects crammed with lies—which are tie only ‘proofs
that W.A. is uniquely auriferous. The p’oof that it isn it,
is that it didn’t produce £1,000,000 worth of gold in its
tenth year.
* * *
There is a legend that several smart, out-of-
work canvassers once got ahead of an insurance society
for a tidy sum by a very simple ruse. The co. allowed
commission in certain cases, sa} v , ten times the
first premium handed in by the canvassers from the
assured. Thus in infant insurance, say 3d. weekly is the
premium. The system was for the canvasser to send m
the name of the infant he assured with 3d., the amount of
first premium, and to receive as his “ commish ” from the
co. 2s. 6d., the co. trusting to future payments to make it
all right. The co. in question was doing a big biz. in
infant assurance, which suddenly ceased. It seems the
canvassers had filled in some hundreds of names (genuine
enough), planked down their own threepences, and
cleared 2s. 3d. a head profit from the co. The lapsing of
98 per cent, of the business before a fortnight passed gave
the whole thing away.

Westralian leaseholders and small syndicates
are lamenting. The present stir is in scrip. Many claims
are always on the point of being sold, but in most cases
it fails to come off. A mine is never sold nowadays till
the money is down.
* * * *
Writes a pick-and-drill expert from Westralia to
a Bulletin man : “The locals here wail about the scarcity
of water, and wonder what wouldn’t their great country
be if this district had a few decent rivers, but this lack of
water has been the making of the place. Take it from
me, if there had been plenty of water here hundreds of
well-boomed mines would have duffered-out long ago.”
It is not generally known that Australian diggers
played an important part in opening up the mines in
Mashonaland. In 1864 Karl Mauch brought back accounts
from that region verifying Livingstone’s stories of alluvial
gold washed out by the natives. People laughed ; but a
party of Australians thought the thing worthy of a trial,
and in ’67 a band of them went north and set up a small
battery on the Tete (near Zambesi). This was the first
serious attempt in modern times at gold-mining in South
Africa.
• * * *
A miner, in recommending to N.S.W. the
M.L. custom of fines for evasion of labor conditions
instead of forfeiture, makes a very sensible suggestion.
Insecurity of tenure is the curse of the industry. And when
a lease is forfeited the State should at least share the
plunder with the jumper.
* * * *
A recent English decision (Paterson v. Gas Light
Co.) has much interest for gas-consumers. It shows
clearly that an incoming tenant is not liable for the arrears
of gas incurred by the preceding tenant, unless by virtue
of an agreement between them. The case was one for an
injunction to restrain the Gas Co. from cutting off the
supply on default of payment of said arrears. Australian
cities are not altogether unacquainted with this develop-
ment of the gas-fiend.

* * * *
An applicant fora junior clerical position (i.e.,
office-boyship) in any Sydney bank nowadays undergoes
much ordeal ere the billet heaves in view. The formal
letter of application requires to be personally substantiated
by one or two well-known citizens, customers of the par-
ticular bank preferred. Then a letter to the secretary of
the Bankers’ Institute is handed by the bank-manager to
the cowering candidate, authorising his examination by
the Institute, and directing that official to forward the
result to the B.M. The C.C. presents this, and is notified
to attend upon a certain day, the field of examination
being specified. However, on arrival of due date he again
attends, and is then requested to fork over £1 for exam.-
fne, no previous intimation of this charge having been
given. The despairing youth hastens forth, returns with
this sum and his stock of lore re English history, geo-
graphy, English composition, French, arithmetic, &c.,
and is then inspected. Then, should rival applicants
have been bested, he is, after further formality, duly ap-
pointed to the position upon three months’ probation,
after which he draws a salary of £25 for an indefinite
period. Truly, bank-billets are not what they were before
the boom.
Dear Bulletin, —Be the “age of consent,”
a long medical experience tells me that the older
(within certain limits) a woman or girl is the more
easy she is to seduce. Therefore, would it not be
more logical to enact that—instead of, as at pre-
sent, under 16 —no woman shall be competent to
give consent over the age of 25 or 30 ? This would
cover everything.—Yours, F.R.C.S.

The best X-ray story yet to hand. Inven-
tor Edison recently received a letter-
Mr. Thomas A. Edison, Menlo Park, N.J.—Dear Sir : I
write you to know if you can make me an X-ray apparatus
for playing against faro banks. I would like to have it •so
I could wear it on my body and have it attached to spec-
tacles or goggles, so I can tell the second card on a deck
of playing-cards turned face up. If you will make it for
me, let me know what it will cost. If I make a success
out of it I will pay you 5000dol. extra in one year. Please
keep this to yourself. If you can’t make it, will you be
kind enough to give me Professor Roentgen’s address ?
Please let me hear from you at once.
Jim was a shearer—a jolly young fellow
whom everybody liked. But he wasn’t called Jim
—“ Let-a-fellow-sleep ! ” was his name ; for, lazy
snoozer ! the phrase was always in his mouth.
They had just cut out at W ,on the Barwon ;
and Jim, wanting to make a daylight start and
knowing his weakness, had asked the boys to call
him bright and early. They did. “Gome on,
turn out, sleepy ! ” “ Here, ‘ Let-a-fellow ’ ! Jim !
wake up ! ” “My God ! Jim’s dead !” _ Then
someone remembered having heard him say
drowsily, in the middle of the night, “ Stop it,
chaps ! let a fellow sleep ! ” They found the snake
—a black one—coiled up in the blankets. Poor
Jim ! He lies just opposite the woolshed, on the
sunrise side. There is a stake with a board nailed
across, and on the board is painted with tar and
turps: “ Jim, shearer, aged 21. Let a fellow
sleep.”
A blind Melbourne man, notable for the
facility with which he traverses the crowded
streets, says that the sense of smell is his chief
guide to locality. He can easily detect the odor of
restaurants, tailors’, fruiterers’, tobacconists’ and
other shops, and can even distinguish between the
city’s most fashionable cafe and other restaurants,
and between certain of the hotels. The sense of
feeling is also used ; the material of which the
building is composed being a guide. Sound is a
further aid, the passing traffic having a different
ring, as the buildings are low or lofty. On a
Sunday, when the shops are closed, he experiences
greater difficulty in finding his way about.
Dear Bulletin, —In my native comer of
Britain there is a particularly sturdy brand of
Presbyterian who mostly refuses to “ kiss the
book,” and for whom the oath-administrator, as
the witness raises his right hand, makes the ad-
dendum : “Do you consider this form binding on
your conscience ? ” And, with the answer “yes !”
the witness is considered duly sworn. The cere-
mony is probably just as futile as kissing a bit of
greasy leather, but there’s a dignified simplicity
about it that makes it look like a better guarantee
of veracity. And, anyhow—putting aside the dirt
and danger of the Bible-mumbling mummery-
will any believer therein explain—l: Would a
torn-out leaf invalidate the oath ? 2: If not,
would a single remaining leaf carry the whole
weight of the Wurrd ? 3 : If not, again, at what
precise number of pages, or lines, or words, does
hell-fire for the false-witness begin ?—Pagan.
With farther reference to a paragraph in
The Bulletin of June 6, based on statements
which Q. ex-Chief Justice Lilley was reported to
have made in Brisbane Courier of May 26, to
the effect that a letter published as from him in
Sydney Daily Telegraph of May 23 was “not one
consecutive letter, but a compilation from several
letters ” which he had written to Mr. J. T. Ryan,
and was “apparently a free condensation ” of his
letters : The Bulletin is assured on behalf of
Mr. Ryan that “the letter published was an exact
copy of Sir Charles Lilley’s letter ” to him, “ and
the matter was contained in one complete letter.”
The Bulletin accordingly withdraws unre-
servedly the paragraph referred to, and tenders to
Mr. Ryan its sincere apology for the paragraph’s
appearance.

At Melbourne, on Monday, that dangerous
and notorious criminal, Butler, who had been
acquitted of robbery-under-arms, but had pleaded,
guilty to housebreaking, was sentenced to 15 years’
penal servitude with periods of solitary confine-
ment. Prisoner, on leaving the dock, exclaimed
that the sentence was brutal and iniquitous
which, in at least one respect, it, in The
Bulletin’s opinion, emphatically was: for when
society has safely caged its human tiger, now
beyond middle age, for a term the expiry of which
will see him a grey-haired if not a decrepit
man, why should it torture him with
solitary confinement ? Then the Judge, after
rebuking Butler for his exclamation, said that
THE PRISONER BUTLER.
lie had only meant to pass a sentence of ten
years —which the prisoner will now serve. Butler,
it is said, was at one period of his life a school-
master ; he is a man of rare but perverted natural
ability, and has on more than one occasion dis-
played such talent in the conduct of his own
defence as to make it more than clear that, under
other circumstances, he might easily have become
an ornament to the Bar. Rarely has there been
seen behind the spikes in any country a man
endowed with such cynical eloquence and facility of
generalisation. Apropos, it is interesting to note,
re Butler’s remark that “people who part their
hair in the middle usually have weak minds,”
that Butler himself, at or about the time he was
tried in Dunedin (1(5 years ago) for the murder of
the Dewars, wore his hair parted in the middle as
shown in the accompanying old photo.

It is reported that the Venezuela frontier-
dispute may, after all, be submitted to arbitration.
At present Britain has five chronic disputes in
South and Central America. One is with Ar-
gentina, from which the dear old mother-country
snavelled the Falkland Islands. One is with
Venezuela, over the ever-extending boundaries of
British Guiana. There is also a dispute with
Brazil over the southern boundary of Guiana, and
another over the island of Trinidad, which was
grabbed a few months ago. And the fifth
dispute is with Guatemala, which is being slowly
eaten up by the unaccountable expansion of British
Honduras. A sixth difficulty is preparing on the
Mexican-British Honduras border, which is also
undefined, and keeps moving. British colonies, in
regions like these, grow by their own volition.
Fifty years ago the British in Guiana persuaded
the Brazilians to move out of a little forlorn border-
town, then claimed by both parties, and arbitrate
over the ownership of it. Now the British frontier
has moved lOOmiles beyond that old, unsettled fron-
tier, and the border-line is still undecided. If the
Brazilians don’t get a definite settlement and build
a stone wall along the border, and put 1760 soldiers
along each mile of wall to watch that it doesn’t
shift, the British frontier will gradually wander
southward till it reaches Rio Janeiro.

At the dinner recently given to Gov.
Hampden at Grafton only 22 local residents could
be induced to buy a ticket at 10s. Gd., though
seven times as many had been catered for. How-
ever, Grafton doesn’t know much about hhdi-
toned banquets. One feature of the feast was an
old gentleman who mixed custard with pickled
beetroot, and recommended it to his neighbors in
a very loud voice as being “damned good.”

Miss Buxton, married at Adelaide to Rev.
Bertram Hawker, son of the late George Hawker,
last week, is the third of Gov. Buxton’s family to
marry a clergyman ; and the other unmarried
daughter is engaged to the piously-inclined
Capt. Guise, of lEE.’s staff. Bridegroom Hawker
is so delicate that he cannot go through the fatigue
of preaching a sermon—a fault which has made
him popular in the sacred city. The couple, how-
ever, will not depend upon pew-rents for their
bread and butter, as Mr. Hawker is heavily gilded
and his bride received a handsome dot.
* * * *
The wedding-gift of the future will not be
counted on as a realizable asset. Some recent
local fashionable marriages have shown that this
system of social blackmailing is falling to pieces.
Hem-stitched doyleys and photo.-frames are now
held even by relatives as an equivalent for an in-
vitation to the obsequies and a nibble at the bridal
show-bread, when formerly no guest would feel
honorably franked for the ceremony by anything
under a plated egg-frame or a whole case of tea-
spoons.

* * * *
The N.S.W. legal profession is gradually
being weeded of defaulters. At this time last
year, seven solicitors were awaiting partial or
total suspension. Now, the Law Institute hasn’t
a single case in hand. But the police are taking
up the running. A brutal outrage on professional
dignity was perpetrated last week. A country
solicitor was actually arrested on a charge of
forging bonds.

A Maoriland storekeeper,
who recently went for a holiday
and never came back, leaving an
immense pile of liabilities be-
hind him, found his banker
rather in the way of his depar-
ture. He had a small overdraft,
and he felt that the all-seeing
eye of the institution was upon
him. So, before leaving, he sent
the bank a brick and some other
trifles done up in a leather case,
and requested it to keep his
jewellery in a safe place till he
came back from a trip down
South. And, even when he
seemed to be rather long r way,
the institution felt so fure that
he wouldn’t have gone : way for
good without his valua les that
it made no inquiry, and held on
to the brick with perfect confid-
ence, and gave him all the start
he wanted.

ONLY LOVE CAN SEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Love is blind, the sages tell us—
He hath ne’er an eye to see,
Ev’ry man he maketh jealous
Over charms that absent be.
False ! Love gives the power of seeing
To the dullest human eye ;
Faintest charm of plainest being
Love’s keen glance will ne’er pass by.
Wisdom’s strong, but Love’s the stronger,
As he ever still must be :
Love is blind ? ’Twill hold no longer,
For ’tis only Love can see.
ULOOLA.

As the age of consent will soon be a burn-
ing question in Australian Parliaments a glance
at the legal marriage ages of Europe may enable
advocates on both sides of the important theme to
buttress their theories with statistical facts. For
instance, in Switzerland a man at 14 may marry a
woman at 12. In Spain, Greece and Portugal the
same law holds good. In Hungary a Catholic
man at 14 may marry a Catholic woman at 12, but
if Protestants the man must be 18 and the woman
15. In Belgium the man must be 18 and the
woman 15. In Austria both must be 14. In Ger-
many the man must be 18 and the woman 15 ; in
France the man 18 and the woman 15; and in
Turkey both at puberty.
Miss Janet Michie, a foremost Melbourne
woman’s rights advocate, writes in the Age of
“ those unnatural men who oppose us.” This is a
turning of the tables on the part of the short-
haired women and long-haired men. The general
idea was that the unnatural man was the long-
haired sort that shrieked for “ woman’s rights.
True there is now a class of sweet girl graduates
on the woman’s rights platform whom any natural
man would wish to support with all his arms—it is
the ancient order, which passeth, that he finds
insupportable.
* * * *
A man who was in the thick of the “Bruns-
wick riots ” at Melbourne the other Sunday says
that the only interesting thing he heard was
spoken by a local publican when sticks were going
quick and lively. “Oh murther ! murther! the
shame ’av it,” he said — c< the black shame that we
should have to close up our bars on a blessed day
like this.”
• * * *
The three policemen who escorted ex-detec-
tive Kirby and his nephew from Sydney to Maon-
land had an awkward time. They were cooped
up in a steerage cabin, and, as neither of the
prisoners would go on deck to he stared at, the
officers had to remain with them continuously.
The watch was all the more strict in view of the
sensational Gasparini escape a few years ago.
* * * *
The New Guinea Government yacht Merrie
England sees a great variety of service. The other
day she annihilated a native tribe at 1 ongari,
conveyed a murderer to the gallows at Samarai ;
and finally landed a party of native convicts to
make roads for a gold-mining co. at Sud-Est. the
“ Merrie England.” Again, the eternal fitness of
things! #

• * * *
Papa Pinnock, P.M., of Brisbane, jubi-
lates over the improved state of that city s morals.
According to the P.M., 70,000 people rejoiced m,
Brisbane on Gov. Lamington’s arrival, and only
two drunks toed the mark at the Police Court
next morning. Mr. Pinnock’s jubilation is ill-
founded—he is simply rejoicing over the fact that
Brisbane has mighty few sixpences to spare tor
beer. The prosperous worker will have his liquor.
Let the Towers, Croydon, and Coolgardie bear
witness. _ .
* * .
A Sydney church has been presented with
the figure of a saint, life-sized, and dressed in the
robes of office. An arrangement of wires enables
mouth and eyes to open and shut and the hands
to gesticulate. The holy fathers are somewhat
afraid of the uncanny gift, which has come to them
all the way from Germany, and, so far, they
exhibit it to friends only.
* * * *
One of the most popular of Melb. girls,
Miss Marie Torrance, was married last week to
Mr. Geo. Lyne, who has brought gold from the
West. Among the Yic. artists Miss Torrance has
long reigned as a sort of innocent Trilby ; her
pretty, vivacious face painted by Fox was seen m
Paris Salon last year, and the picture was pre-
sented by the artists to the bride as a wedding-
gift. 4 ,
* * * *
The “ladies” of Bundaberg (Q.), who feed
their “ slaveys ” on 21 meals of fried liver per
week, are agitating for the renewal of servant-girl
immigration. They want servants, willing to
accept cheap wages, imported at State expense,
and they ask the hon. member for Simpson (elected
through an informality in his opponent’s nomina-
tion-paper) to put the matter through for them.

“ One pound for marriage—one pound for
divorce. Nothing for marriage nothing for
divorce. I call that fair,” yelled a pot-hatted
Sydney Domain orator one Sunday lately. “ Your
barbarous law proves itself a hideous man-trap, m
that it lets a man in for a pound and won’t let
him out—unless he’s a pauper —under fifty. Ive
been there ; and I’ll never rest till your laws are
reformed in this direction. ”

* * *
Australians show
marked preference for
divorce outright as com-
pared or contrasted with
judicial separations.
When, however, these
latter take place, griev-
ances of a bitter kind
frequently arise. When
the wife obtains her
separation she discovers,
as in some recent cases,
that the maintenance
allowed her is far below
what she expected. The
Court’s finding seems to
her to depend more on
the state of its liver than
on any reasonable calcu-
lation of income and out-
lay. Of course, husbands’
incomes vary; but in-
stead of ordering a man
to pay so much per week,
the Court might bind
him to pay a certain
fixed proportion of his
income.
H. : “ Yesterday the train in which I was
ran over and killed a poor little girl, while it
was slowing into the station at the gates. ihe
most pitiful thing ever I saw. The people all
looking out of the windows, while the mangled,
bleeding sacrifice was laid on a stretcher. .Some-
one comes with her little cap,- another with a shoe.
A white quilt is fetched from the gate-cottage to
cover her. The wooden-legged gatekeeper, him-
self once the victim of a similar accident, protests
‘ I does all I can to stop ’em-.’ ‘ Whose child is
it?’ is asked; the little mates reply; and away
goes the stretcher to mother, the victim taking the
news herself, as it were, in this awful way.”

In a very pious Australian drinlc-cure
asylum, a newly-received inmate, suffering fiom
“horrors,” asked for a tonic. “I have only
got three sorts of physic,” said the manager, ancl
you can have which you like. There’s castor-oil.
“No, thanks.” “There’s Epsom salts?
“Faugh!” “And there’s chloride of lime!
“ Wha-a-t! ” . . . But, so runs the legend, a
change shortly came over the spirit of the dream.
One after the other came along other patients to
see the new man, and each _ asked— “ Haven’t you
had a nip this morning ? Nothing ? No .1 oo .
You ask So-and-so ; he’ll get you one, and tell the
manager to go to glory ! We pay our own way
here, and we’re going to have a time ot it.

* * ——
Melb. cabmen are appealing to Parliament
for protection : they want a law to enable them to
deal promptly with the man who won t pay. Is
the tribe deteriorating ? Hitherto cabby has had a
very effective way of dealing with this sort. Ihe
writer once saw and heard a Melb. cabby curse a
stockbroker from the Town Hall to the Exchange.
“ And all for sixpence ! ” somebody remarked.
“ Yes, mate,” replied the crimson Jehu, but it s
the blue, blanky principle of the thing.
Cabbies in general should demand some
such legislation as that now before the British
Parliament, dealing with the obnoxious hirer who
either can’t pay or won’t pay. Under the British
Bill cabby will be empowered to demand proof
that the hirer has money enough in his pocket to
pay. Further, if a hirer doesn’t pay he may be
arrested and held until cash is forthcoming, when,
in addition to the fare, he will also have to pay
40s. for the trouble given.
A ROTORUA LOVE SONG.
Oh, dearest, darkest maiden, my heart is calling
thee,
Come forth from out thy whare, and have a bath
with me.
The ti-tree waves a welcome; the half-moon soft
doth shine;
The sulphur fumigates the air as thy flat nose
meets mine.
All day while catching crayfish thine image went
with me —
I thought how in the warm bath I’d eat them raw
with thee.
Then come and have thy bath, love, and see the
half-moon shine,
And, eat the jucy kuras, and rub thy nose with
mine.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
LtcstTM “ A Trip to Chinatown.”
Royal “Trilby” Burlesque.
Tivoli Riokards’ Variety Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “A Gilded Fool.” £
Royal “ Cheer, Boys, Cheer?’
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Town Hall Trebelli Concerts.
The second edition of the “ Trip to China-
town ” went up at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday
night, and will fill in the last two weeks of the long
season. . The difference between the new edition
and the old one lies in the musical part of the
show —the frivolity itself frivols just the same as
before. Prank Lawton, the heavenly whistler of
the co., now toots an entirely new series of melodies
which are, if possible, better than the old ones.
Lawton’s whistle is an oasis which is capable of
brightening a whole Sahara of burlesque; he makes
amends for the commonplace of the stout tenor and
the ordinariness of the stout baritone, and for the
painful resemblance of these two stout people to
each other, and for many other things besides.
The audience didn’t really want a new edition of
Lawton—the old one was good enough for any-
body ; but it accepts it with thanks all the same.
The other changes are mostly for the better, and
the signs are that “ Chinatown ” will boom to the
bitter end of the season. Mrs. Brown Potter and
the ambrosial Curly B’lew are due at the Lyceum
on the Bth. They strike Sydney at a good time
when competition is at itswery lowest and shows
are almost as scarce as bunyips on Pharaoh’s
pyramid, and if they don’t boom exceedingly
under these conditions, then there is no good in
them, and Curly is a lost and hopeless Bellew.
* * *
Bland Holt runs the “Woman’s Revenge ”
at Sydney Royal until Monday night, on which
date the bloodshot eye of Cosgrove will finally fix
Norman through the window, and Miss Watson
will be triumphantly acquitted, and the wicked
attorney will fall in the soup of retribution, all for
the very last time. After that Bland removes his
vast show to Brisbane for a three weeks’ season,
and the evergreen Maggie Moore takes possession
of the Royal on Wednesday with her “Trilby”
burlesque, the Franco-Irish maiden with the un-
dressed feet being played, of course, by Maggie
herself. This is understood to be really and truly
Miss Moore’s farewell till she comes back again,
and as a guarantee of good faith a copy of her pas-
senger ticket by the Mariposa is ad vertised on the
programme. The lady also advertises that she
comes with a specially-selected co. and a grateful
heart. The co. has already been tried in Mel-
bourne and pronounced good, and Maggie’s ad-
mirers in Sydney will take the grateful heart for
granted. When the “Trilby” season is over,
Bland Holt returns to his old quarters and pro-
poses to let loose a whole series of new plays on a
staggered public. He has about a dozen on hand,
and in at least eight of them the British flag
waves gloriously over the battle-field while the red
sun goes down on heaps of slain—after which the
hero proves his innocence, or finds the lost docu-
ment, or recovers his ancestral mansion, or some-
thing.
** # *
The Frantz Family turned up at Sydney
Tivoli on Saturday, and swept the audience off its
fc et with sheer enthusiasm. In the great art and
mystery of india-rubber bounding there is abso-
-1 itely nothing which seems to come amiss to the
Frantzes. They are the boundingest combination
this paper knows of, and they appear to be inde-
pendent of gravity and cohesion and everything
else that is possible to dispense with —except
clothes. They swallow themselves and spit them-
selves out again frequently, and any one of them
can fall up on to nothing at the shortest notice.
Also, each Frantz climbs on top of himself, and
then hauls himself up with his hands and climbs
up again. The Family is presumably an orphan,
its male parent having taken a high jump some
years ago, and not come down since. There are
three ladies and four gentlemen in the combina-
tion, which is a high-toned affair and turns somer-
saults in evening dress. If anything in these
remarks seems to approach towards fiction it must
be set down to pressure of necessity, The Bulle-
tin having used up all the facts about this remark-
able crowd during its Melbourne season. Taking
them all round they seem to about exhaust the
possibilities of acrobatism, and the next Family
which comes along in that line of business wiil
have to hustle considerably if it wants to make
bread and cheese.
** * *
Gill, the rowdy person who fell out of the
gallery at Sydney Tivoli while trying to jump
somebody else’s front-seat, and fell fatally on top
of Alfred Neander, was last week found guilty of
manslaughter and sentenced to six months. Con-
sidering the hobnailed rowdyism of the case,
the public wastes little sympathy over Mr.
Grill. Further, he was ordered to enter
into his own recognisances to be of good be-
haviour and not fall on top of anyone else for a
period of 12 months ; so, if he does fall on anyone
within that period, his recognisances will be for-
feited. As he probably has no earthly use for his
recognisances, and never knew until now that he
possessed any, the chances are that he doesn’t care
much. The simple faith of the bench in recog-
nisances as a means of putting down rowdyism is
touching and beautiful to behold.
** * *
Rev. Charles Clark, having done the wilds
of Crocodile Land as far as Cairns and the
Barron Falls, is now working his way back to
Sydney and lecturing to the local cockatoo as he
goes. He commences a farewell series of lectures
in Sydney en 6th August; another in Melbourne
on the 22nd, and the very last ef all in Adelaide
on 11th September. After which the Rev. Clark
—the best lecturer who ever struck this country,
and the only man who ever made a pile by lectur-
ing in Australia —moves Englandwards, with no
expectation of ever coming back..

Chevalier de Kontski, the veteran piano-
nobleman, gives two farewell concerts at Sydney
Town Hall on Wednesday afternoon and Saturday
(12th and 15th August).
** * *
Nat Goodwin, supported by his company
from America, made a first appearance at Melb.
Princess’s on Saturday. The test-piece chosen for
this occasion is said to be one of the most popular
in Mr. Goodwin’s repertoire, and, insofar that its
name-part enabled him to prove his quality before
a strange audience, “A Gilded Fool ” was a suc-
cess on Saturday night. The character of the
G.F. is neither novel, nor well-constructed, nor
anyway fascinating, but the comedian obtains a
strong compound of humor and pathos from it.
Clearly, Nat Goodwin is an excellent artist, of the
quietest and most natural American type. He
exaggerates nothing, nor ever forces a point, and
he is, for intelligent people, a very acceptable con-
trast to his clever predecessor, Kyrle Bellew. To
the same extent that a polished American gentle-
man is about the “nicest” English - speaking
person of his sex, Nat Goodwin is about the
nicest sort of comic-serio actor that grows.
A less stagey performer of a somewhat impossible
character couldn’t be found. Goodwin’s gilded
fool (who is inconsistently wise at times) presents
a droll appearance and does many absurd things,
consequently his love-scenes, ridiculous to the eye,
need to be made interesting by the suggestion of a
genuine 18-carat heart. He achieves this purpose.
The unheroic Chauncey Short shows himself a
hero; and probably most of the women in the
house feel equal to adoring him at the finish,
despite his unromantic and badly-tailored per-
sonality. The leading lady, Miss Blanche Walsh,
is also an admirable artist who bestows her stage
affections on the funny man with good taste and
feeling. The human interest of the play is con-
fined to this pair of lovers.
* * * *
‘‘ A Gilded Fool ” has a poor little wire-
drawn plot running through the last three acts.
The first act simply introduces Nat Goodwin to
notice as a rather vulgar young capitalist, suffering
a recovery from an overnight drunk, in his bache-
lor’s flat. The bed and bath rooms are shut off
from the reception-room by curtains, and the
bachelor is seen tumbling from his bed to his bath-
reserve in pyjamas, after which he prowls around
in a state of uncompleted toilet. The first 40
minutes are devoted to superfluous dialogue. Then
the unscrupulous elderly-partner in a firm of
stockbrokers schemes to get the gilded fool's
capital into the business to save it from ruin, which
is objected to by the other elderly partner, who
happens to be the father of the girl. How-
ever, the fool pays money into the firm previous
to its collapse, but, at the same time, he makes a
million dollars or so by operating on his own
account, and so he reconstructs his father-in-law
elect and marries the girl, after having got rid of
the unscrupulous partner. It is a weak narrative.
The two grey-haired partners are monotonously
similar in all matters save their sense of honor,
and this one distinction makes no difference in
their acting. Two or three young men hover
around the sordid commercial incident, likewise
two middle-aged ladies, one of whom is our well-
preserved idol of other days, Miss Emelie Melville.
She is a slender idol now, but not much thinner
than at the time of her third visit to Australia.
Anyway, the audience recognised its beloved
Emelie in an instant, and gave her a warm recep-
tion.
* * * ♦
A cheerful little actress, Miss Ethel Brown-
ing, who speaks with a full twang and garbs her
frail figure to the least possible advantage, may be
said to run up and down the love-passages ; but
she, like several of the others, is only a make-
weight item in the cast. As far as one can judge
from a first piece, there is no phenomenal merit in
Mr. Goodwin’s company, apart from himself
and Miss Walsh. The other ladies seem quite
good enough, whilst the heavy gentlemen are cer-
tainly not so. As a whole, the co. doesn’t come
near to the Brough- Boucicault combination ; there-
fore, considering the recent ill-success of B. & B.
in first-class comedies, it wouldn’t be safe to pre-
dict too huge a triumph for “A Gilded Fool.” The
mounting, by G. and J. Gordon, is exceptionally
solid and regardless of expense.

At Melbourne Bijou the experiment of
“ Fun on the Bristol ” will result in “ A Trip to
Chicago,” another of John Sheridan’s musical
farces, on Saturday. A slight enlargement of the
present company will suffice to meet the new bill,
and meanwhile “ The New Housemaid ” is post-
poned indefinitely. Bijou matters are, however,
reported to be a little mixed owing to the indispo-
sition of Miss Marie Luella, and the above
arrangements are to be received with caution.
The Opera House is chiefly depending on
Charles Godfrey just now, previous to a re-
appearance of Carl Hertz, the high-toned
illusionist whom George Musgrove first imported
to Australia some years ago. Concert music has
slowed down in Melbourne for the moment, but on
Saturday a second season of Mdlle. Trebelli,
assisted by a somewhat stronger company than
before, will start at the Town Hall. Also, old
friend Musin, the violinist, is to make melody in
the Athenaeum Hall at an early date.
Maggie Moore’s good-lucky season at Melb.
Royal ends on Friday. To-night (Thursday) that
evergreen appealer to Irish sentiment revives
Struck Oil,” under distinguished patronage, for
the benefit of herself and the public at large. And
to-morrow, probably, the time-honored attraction
will repeat itself by general desire, after which the
departing manageress will speak a few farewell
remarks m the voice that never fails to fetch ’em,
and cover her retreat with a promise to appear
again next year.
** * *
George Rignold the Great takes possession
of the Royal on Saturday, when the long-looked-
for “ Cheer, Boys, Cheer ” is to be set up with a
strong cast and much gorgeous scenery, likewise
a tender regard for necessary adjuncts and minute
details. George Rignold captured this big show
during his recent visit to London, and has been
working at its production like a giant refreshed by
ozone.

At Poverty Point.
The unostentatious return of Emelie Melville with Nat
Goodwin’s co. was the sensation to Melbourneites. And
what a reception she got! She has come back as charm-
ing as ever, though, of course, less voluptuously so, and
as a comedienne is distinctly a success.
Emelie Melville’s return will re vive innumerable memories
of that operatic light of other days. During her prime,
the adorable little woman could never appear in the
streets without having a curious and admiring crowd
gaping after her. She and Gracie Plaisted, too, were
about the first women who donned “ rationals ” in Aus-
tralia on a certain historical trip to Marysville.

with Herr and Mdme. Vogrich (Alice Rees), who are
settled here and doing well —the latter is looking wonder-
fully well and much improved since the old “Patience”
days. lam in negotiation with some very strong attrac-

way, an uncontradicted statement has been made in the

managing-director of the new Palace Theatre, so I have
written in for the scene-painter’s billet.”

Maggie Moore’s benefit at Melb. Royal provided an out-
let, this last week or so, for the furious enthusiasm of
Clifford Ferley, who represents her in front of the house.
Ferley never seems quite happy unless he is acting as
honorary secretary to a testimonial committee. That’s
the young man’s amiable vice. If he ever gets aloft he
will insist upon arranging a benefit for St. Peter, and the
authorities will receive a letter, signed C.F., inviting them
to purchase a bundle of tickets in consideration of the
archangel’s long and faithful services.
Mr. Harry Nathan, organist of St. Mary’s Church
Waverley (Sydney), has been appointed organist of the
Anglican Church at Charters Towers. Mr. Nathan was
one of Montague Younger’s most capable pupils and used
to act for him when necessary. He can sing as well as
play, and his rendering of Glover’s “Consider the Lilies,”
in which his manly baritone revels, is a musical treat.
Miss Edith O’King, a close relative of the well-known
Newcastle organist, hails from the N.S.W. Northern dis-
trict, and is just, now one of Sydney’s favorite sopranos.
She has done good work at St. Patrick’s Church under
the beneficent influence of Wiegand ; and, since Colborne-
Baber’s departure, has practically taken her position as
the principal local soprano.
Bellew and Potter during this Melbourne season put up
at Middle Park, and, taking-the air on the seashore as
they did daily, they were a spectacle for the gods. ’ La
Potter looked limp and eerie, with storm-tossed hair, and
wore eccentric garments suggesting the idea that Cora
had just escaped from a fire and was looking for someone
to button her up, and over all was an outre Bernhardtian
abandon that knocked the droves of bicyclists silly and
all the whirled wondered. Bellew, too, wore clothes’ that
never were on land or sea, and an assumed and interesting
pallor, and a quantity of grey hair becomingly dashed
back. The pair were sicklied o’er with the pale cast of
advt.., and, seeing them, one thought only of a mad
angular poster by. Aubrey Beardsley, inviting attention to
the fact that “ Camille ” was being played nightly—prices
as usual. 1

Wallace Brownlow has joined the digger-ranks at
Hannan’s. The local Athletic Club gave him a compli-
mentary social the other Sunday evening, at which the
handsome baritone sang no less than five songs.
The “Trilby” eight-night season at Adelaide Royal
beat all records. On four evenings every stall and circle
seat was reserved.
George Bryer, who longtime looked after things for his
relative Willie Edouin, at the Strand Theatre, London,
has been managing the Luella-Cosgrove show at Melbourne
Bijou. Bryer came to Australia last summer with a
few comedies and light musical pieces in his bag, but he
doesn’t seem to believe overmuch in the land of his birth,
and talks of getting back to London shortly. Willie
Edouin, at latest dates, was touring the English provinces
in a new musical comedy called “Newmarket.”
Brisbane lately had six shows all running at once :
“Trilby,” Brough’s, Holloway’s, English Concert Co.,
Fitzgeralds’ circus, and Speed’s variety show.
F. Barron Morley, the son of Fred. Morley, organist of
St. John’s Church, Darlinghurst, and nephew of the
eminent English composer of same surname, is one of the
most promising of young pianists. He is not unlike Mark
Hambourg in abruptness of style, and with his father’s
practical advice and assistance bids fair to make a name.
He will shortly tour Australia. Sydney Liedertafel has
already started to acknowledge the young artist’s excep-
tional abilities.
There’s something objectionable in the platform de-
meanor of at least two lady vocalists who appear at Mel-
bourne concerts when the Brasseys are about. They have
a habit of favoring His Ex. and co. with a private and
confidential bow of recognition, which suggests that they
are about to warble for the sole benefit of those exalted
acquaintances, and don’t care a button for the vulgar
herd. One of the ladies in particular takes a jaunty pose
on the platform, with her hands behind her back, tips a
liberal smile to her friends and relatives in the front seats,
and then proceeds to catch the eye of the Gov'nor’s party,
way up in the balcony. Having captured the vice-regal
optic, mdlle. jerks her head in an easy here-we-are-again
style, and trills her little lay. The vulgar herd feels as
though it were intruding on a “ sassiety At Home.”
Among concert-goers there is a growing feeling against
the stupid old custom of artists leaving the platform after
a song, when they might as well retire behind a screen,
and so save a lot of time in coming back to take “ re-
calls ” and encores. Neither is there any reason why the
accompanist shouldn’t stay at the piano, except during
the 10 minutes interval. Concerts are made almost in-
tolerable by waits between songs, more wait between the
song and the recall, and again between the recall and the
encore, which last wait is prolonged by an interval
between the return of the pianist to the piano, and the
arrival of the lady or gent who has “ yielded to the
wishes of her (or his) admirers.” The public are accus-
tomed to get their encores quickly in a grand opera per-
formance, and the foolery associated with second-rate
warbling of trumpery ballads makes them very tired.
Let some enterprising concert-manager advertise : “ No
running up and down stairs between numbers. Carriages
at 9.30,” and try the effect.

At some of the English theatres they serve two courses
of “ early door.” Say the performance starts at 7.30 p.m.
and the minimum price for pit seats is a “bob”—from
6.15 to 6.30 there is a half-crown “ early door,” and from
6.30 to 7.15 the charge is a florin. In the common event
of the'pit being filled a quarter of an hour before curtain-
rise the “ bob ” admission fee doesn’t get a look in.

Captain Randell, who takes the place of
the suddenly - ejected Willcox as member for
Gumeracha (S.A.) is a tough and ancient.democrat
of 72 years, and is alleged to be the first man,
barring the aboriginal, who ran any vessel better
than a dug-out or a sheet of bark on the Murray.
A few years ago the ‘ ‘ Mickeys ’ of Ivyne-
ton and district (Vic.),, armed with whisky and
big sticks, were holding a meeting m their
hall; while the Orangemen met m their
room over the way ; and both were rusty
for a fight. Suddenly the door of the
“ Green ” room opened, and a little
old man entered and turned the
key behind him. The mob
opened out; the little man
passed up on to the platform
amid a heavy silence. “ Bhoys,
said the little man, “ I married
most of your parents; I chris-
tened all of ye. The boys that
love me, pass up their weapons
and go home. Those that don t,
stay.” Every stick was passed
up, and every boy went home,
Then the little man—Father
Geoghegan, since dead, who had
lived 40 years or more round Kyne-
ton —walked over to the Orange hall.
What he said was never known, but
the procession did not walk that day.

James West, of Macquarie Plains, Bathurst
(N S W.), died last week, aged 100 years. He
arrived in the district in 1821, the year after Mac-
quarie visited the locality and named the town.

Ovens. Papa speaks of the Attorney-General as
“Mr. Isaacs,” and of the other as “my son Jack.

firm of Southwell Coultas (Dim.). Which reminds

Robert Frazer, patentee of the automatic
door controller (for railway carriages in particular)
was bridegroom at a swell Melbourne wedding
the other day—and now has two controllers
instead of one. Mrs. Frazer, who brings money
into the partnership, will accompany her husband
and his invention to Europe.

his still-quoted definition of a Scotch haggis a

The success of the appeals to the Full
Bench from Chief Justice Madden’s decisions, is
becoming quite a scandal. Hartley Williams rubs
his hands. Molesworth, senior, rounded on
Stawell, Barry, and Williams, senior, by getting
their reversals reversed by the Privy Council.
Doth Madden await his revenge this way !
Mr. Dan. Cooper, son of the Sydney
baronet, is said to be the first Australian included
among the members of the Jockey Club invited
to the Prince of Wales’ Derby day dinner at
Marlborough House. If rumor speaks truly,
Daniel has paid for his whistle with a vengeance :
To keeping company with Albert Edward is an
expensive business.

Madden has two brothers, Frank and Walter, and
two brothers-in-law, Frank and Sydney Stephen,
nil four practising as solicitors. Judge A’Beckett s
sin William G., and his nephew, T. A’Beckett
Weigall, are barristers. Judge Casey has a

while Judges Williams, Holroyd, Hood, Gaunt,
Chomley and Molesworth all have sons in active
practice.

Jermilky Jerrum, in his To-day, alleges
that a Brisbane correspondent informs him that
he has “ been paying too much attention to the
opinions of what he calls ‘ that scurrilous rag, The
Sydney Bulletin.’” Considering the literary
matter that Jerome has stolen from The Bulletin
and used as his own without acknowledgment, it
may fairly be said that he has been “paying too
much attention ” to it.
The long-maned teetotal evangelist, Tenny-
son Smith, once remonstrated with a Melb. sub-
editor for ignoring his cold-tea campaign. The
scribe replied that as Mr. Smith was a showman
he should advertise. Mr. Smith retorted that he
was not a showman —he worked for the Cause for
the redemption of humanity. _ Whereupon the
scribe slewed his chair round with : Well, if you
are not a showman, why don’t you get your hair
cut ? ”
Dowager Countess Fortescue, who died in
London the other day, could look back through
the mists of 71 years to her first wedding, and she
was 20 when that happened. She had two hus-
bands and no children. The Bulletin mentions
Countess Fortescue as an encouragement for Aus-
tralian girls to marry into the British peerage,
where womankind approaches nearer and nearer
to immortality. “Damme, some of ’em never
die ! ” said the observant Maior Pendennis.
B. R. Wise, Charles Lyne and Miss Annie
T. Parkes should have held a friendly session and
compared their literary materials, with a view to
producing one comprehensive life of the note-
worthy old man. As it is, the three books—all
Sydney efforts, are likely to overlap one another
in various respects, and are almost certain to b©
contradictory of one another’s statements. Besides,
not one hundred people will buy three lives of
Parkes published in one year, or three books of
any kind about Parkes.
Governor Gormanston (Tas.) likes to shoot
and fish in the country incog. He recently put
up for a night or two at a small place of casual
entertainment, kept by an old countrywoman of
his own. In the morning, he found that his mud-
covered boots were uncleaned, and he told the
landlady that, though he was in the country, he
liked his boots polished all the same. Un,
indade,” sez the ould woman. “ I dar say, whin
ver at home ye clane thim yerself, an ye can do
th’ same now ! ” The next morning, Jemco put
his foot on a hen-coop, and cleaned his boots him-
self.

The Sergeant-at-Arms of Tas. Assembly
is the only man in these parts who looks
the thing. He carries arms—an old
theatrical “ property ” sword—and has
the general appearance of having seen
some awful fight and come out bat-
tered beyond recognition. People,
that the Sergeant was the model that
Howard Vernon took for dm make-
up of the old warrior in Ma Mie
Rosette.” He stands 6ft. 5in., is
long and lean, wears spectacles, also
a pair of green goggles, and a shade
over both eyes, or a patch over one.
Indeed the Sergeant might step
straight into “Ma Mie” and wipe
out Vernon in point of appearance
and make-up.

In matter-of-fact America, where they have
buildings 20 storeys high, it is found impossible to

Melbourne police are prosecuting two men
who passed a bogus bank-note on a fortune-teller
in payment for information about the future.
This looks illogical. If the fortuneteller was a
genuine prophet with power to see into the un-
seen, she must have known that her clients were
trying to swindle her and have taken the note
with that knowledge, and as she presumab y
wouldn’t have taken a bad note knowingly the
natural assumption is that she didn t take it at all.
If she did take it unknowingly, then she is
obviously a sham prophet and should be run m tor
trving to get money under false pretences. ine
seer who can’t tell a bad note from a good one
doesn’t deserve much sympathy, anyhow.

The R.C. doctrine of Purgatory is in close
touch with the Theosophical theory of re-inoama-
tion. According to the priest a soul must work
out its own salvation in one purgatorial period,
and according to the old-new philosophy the same
thing is accomplished through a more or less long
series of earthly lives. Had Mrs. Besant been m
Melbourne a few weeks ago, when father Isaao
Moore delivered his denunciation of the tailao,
that Repentant Fat-men can comfortably fall
asleep in the Lord,” a luminous controversy upon
the rival merits of Purgatory and Re-incarnation
might have given a shake-up to Australian irre-
ligious thought. It is worth remarking that the
most bigoted Roman Oatholios and the most ad-
vanced agnostics agree in ridiculing the cardinal
doctrine of Protestantism, whereby a converted
murderer is adjudged capable of being hurled
straight to Abraham’s bosom by a jerk of Nosey
Bob’s lever.
That ‘ ‘ Barcoo rot ” has
its origin in splinters or dust
from certain scrubs getting into
scratches or slight sores has long
been suspected, but never pro-
ven. An interesting support of
this conjecture is that doctors
have recently noted amonjj
workers in teak timber a curi-
ous inflammation of the skin,
and named it “ teak-disease.
This inflammation followed by
suppuration has been expert
mentally shown to be caused
by small splinters piercing the
skin, or teak-dust setting on
abraded surfaces, such experi-
ments confirming the belief of
the teak - workers themselves.
“Barcoo rot ” might well be
similarly investigated.

POINTS
Insolvent members are now sitting both in
N.S.W. Council and Assembly.
Hancock’s definition of Vic. Labor-party :
“ Pace-makers to the Turner Ministry.”
There is neither a Roman Catholic nor an
Irishman in the present N.S.W. Ministry. But
Premier Reid professes to dearly love Tipperary
Dan.
Viator: “Australian loyalty to England
is geographical. Like a pudding where the plums
sink to the bottom. Warmest in South Australia
Proper, coldest in South Australia Improper —Car-
pentaria!
There are within Sydney metropolitan
area 42 clubs retailing liquor and paying no license.
How will Local Option get at these? If Local
Option shut up 40 pubs., will not 40 additional
clubs take their places?
It may be right sometimes to crawl, and it
may be all right sometimes to fight; but mingled
fight and crawl is a sorry spectacle. The sight of
a minister of the crown being dragged around by
the boom-press with all the fight bumped out of
him is too despicable for words.
Thus the Argus, July 13 :
A fiscal system is not to be judged by either six weeks
or six months of its existence. Six years would be a
moderate term of experiment.
And then it whoops through a column about the
success of N.S.W. Freetrade for the last half-year.
Old-man Parkes seems to have kept every
letter he ever received, and amongst his corre-
spondence have been found epistles from several
N.S.W. Labor-members promising to support the
want-of-confidence resolution he moved against
Reid. Which letters may possibly make a public
appearance just before next general election.
How railway deficits are made. On aQ.
suburban line nearly 7000 stoppages are made to
pick up and set down about 950 passengers. Depart-
mental figures give the cost of stoppages at about
£3O, and fares .£6O, but the actual cost of stop-
pages seems to be about =£27o. A railway official
is said to own a hunk of land near the station.
In Sydney to-day over 2000 families, total-
ling about 10,000 human beings, are receiving
bread from the Benevolent Asylum each week.
Even then single men have to be refused in most
cases, in order that the married destitute with
children may be fed. No wonder the Benevolent
Asylum report has been suppressed for political
purposes.
The PuNCH-like attempts of the Anglo-
Australian daily press to be funny about the “ Boy
Orator of Nebraska’s ” nomination as U.S. Presi-
dent prove conclusively that the writers have never
heard of the clause in the U.S. Constitution which
debars “ boys ” from occupying the Presidential
chair until they have reached the fairly mature
age of 35.

Fink, M.L.A.
The list of public men in Victoria —men who had ad-
ministered the affairs of the country—did not contain the
names of many who had been brilliant successes in finan-
cial matters, whereas it did contain the names of many
who had met their downfall in business-concerns.

H. : “ The Argus is sarcastic on an orator
who, at the Orange-Catholic fight, urged both
sides to drop their nonsense, and join in belting
the monopolist. This is what every newspaper
man knows to be wisdom, but, as Hamlet says, he
does not think it wisdom to be here set down.
While the Uganda niggers fight, as Orangemen
and Catholics, ‘Me Prostan, you Catlick! ’ it
would be unfair not to keep up the balance at this
end of the globe.”
M‘Farlane, M.L.A., has before N.S.W.
Assembly a Bill for the abolition of usury. It
provides that no interest, bonus, or other consider-
ation for money lent shall be lawful if it exceeds
15 per cent. But it doesn’t say as definitely as it
should whether, supposing a man lends £lOO at
40 per cent., and can’t recover his interest privately
through his client’s fear of exposure, he can then
sue for his principal and 15 per cent., or whether
the fact that he has broken the statute makes his
principal and interest both wholly irrecoverable.
Tasmanian Parliament retains the old
joke about introducing a bill. The member
appears at the bar of the House, flourishes a bit
of the Mercury rolled up, or one of Tattersall’s
hand-bills, and yells out, “A bill, Mr. Speaker.”
The Speaker says, “ Bring it up,” and the mem-
ber does, and hands it to the clerk of the House,
who drops it into the W.P.B. The second read-
ing is made an order of the day for that day
week. One day the late clerk, Mr. Manning,
found that “ the bill ” was a butcher’s account be-
ginning, “p. saus., 31b.”
The Maoriland Oppositionists are in a
parlous state. Every day their chances of
ousting Se 4don grow more remote; every
day their tactics become more desperate.
THE RIGHT TIME TO SWEAR OFF.
PARSON : “ When will up the cursed drink?"
TOPER : “ When I can’t (hie) help meself."
They are now stealing the Govt, policy to
make their own platform. They are like their
fellow favorites, who come to a digging after
the Caucasian has deserted it, and make a living
by working over the tailings. Their big gun,
Stout, resembles nothing so much as the geological
phenomenon known as a trapdyke, a spurt of old
lava running at right angles to the line of the
strata. Trapdyke Stout!
After a year’s continuous demands by
Parliament, and three promises that it should be
produced “immediately,” the list of the Reid
Government’s civil service appointments turned
up the other day. The figures show that the
God-fearing and retrenching Ministry made 733
appointments in about 18 months—almost as many
as their predecessors made in three years. And
yet who doesn’t remember the fearsome howl of
THE RUMINATING ANIMAL.
“ Oh, mamma, can I have some more?"
“ There is no more, dear."
“ Oh, mamma, I DO wish I was a cow so that I could chew it all over again."

the earliest appointments is said to be Mr. Reid’s

This year Vic. Leg. Council passed the
Shops and Factories Bill—rejeoted last session—
almost without a murmur. Also, it almost looks
as if the Companies Bill might get through as well
which would be still more surprising-. Apparently
Turner’s policy of saying nothing about the Coun-
cil’s rejection of his measures but peacefully send-
ing them up again and again without comment
and leaving the old men to talk each other to
death over them, is beginning to act. Also
Solicitor-General Cuthbert grovels so beautifully
to the M.’sL.C. that even they get mollified at
times.
The Vic. Government has introduced an
Insolvency Bill, which abolishes the secret-com-
position curse, and the private trustee who gener-
ally bolts with the assets, and most of the other
bad features of the old law. The Turner Ministry
is putting up a good record by tackling the big
questions which its predecessors have shirked for
the last 25 years, and it does its work without
banquets and progresses through the country and
blowing of the big, brass trumpet of advt. If it
lasts four years longer it should about manage to
make Victoria an up-to-date Democratic country.
And by then it will probably begin to wear beauti-
ful towels on its boots and be made a K.C.M.G.
and it will be high time for it to go.
Although 45 years ago N.S. Wales sent to
England a protest specifying among other points
“ that all offices of trust should be conferred on
colonists, except that of Governor,” Deputy
Mint-Master J. Macdonald Cameron, who figured
the other day among the Burns enthusiasts,
was nominated and appointed by England, with-
out any consultation with N.S. Wales, and is paid
monthly by N.S.W. taxpayers.
Brisbane is making desperate
efforts to emulate Sydney and be*
come the port, par excellence, of its
province. It is a mournful but
indisputable fact that poor old
Rockhampton, with its dreadful
sewer of a river, actually beats
Brisbane in the matter of exports,
and Townsville, for all its lighter-
ing, is very little behind. Mean-
while, the true ports of Bananaland,
Gladstone and Bowen and Cairns,
are patiently waiting till the
ancient log-rollers step aside and
give them a chance.
Cabled that “ the French
Chamber of Deputies has rejected
the income-tax proposals intro-
duced by the Radicals by 29 votes.”
Once again France stands out
against a tax which even monarchi-
cal countries have adopted, and
which is accepted by ultra-Liberal
and Liberal economists generally
as a valuable instrument for the
equitable apportionment of State
burdens. But it is historical sen-
timent and not economic science
which in this matter appears to
dominate France. The National
Assembly of 1789, under the sway
of La Rochefoucauld and Talley-
rand, laid it down that “every
system of taxation which necessi-
tates personal and arbitrary inquisi-
tions for its execution is inconsis-
tent with the maintenance of a free
people.” This is identical with the
Australian argument against in-
come-tax. But when France carried
the proposition the tax had become
an inordinately vexatious extor-
tion, and it is possibly a remem-
brance of this extortion which
keeps the proposition alive.
Premier Reid certainly takes a very opti-
mistic view of things if he imagines, as stated in
his budget speech, that the first year’s cost of in-
augurating and collecting N.S. W. land and income
tax will be under 10 per cent., and that the annual
cost will be from 3to 4. Taking the experience of
other places, it may be safely said that 20 per cent,
will be nearer the mark for the first year, and the
annual cost thereafter will be fully 7 per cent. By
the way, do the land and income tax commis-
sioners make any allowance to the postal dept, for
having their letters and papers franked through
the post ?
On Thursday of last week, Premier Reid’S
list of prices of .N.S.W. and Vic. Government
stores was duly circulated in print. Considering
that the said list had been proved several days
previously to be a lie and a delusion, and G. Reid
had already shunted the blame of it on to the
Govt stores official, and that person had acknow-
ledged that lie had somehow taken a wrong list
and that his facts were consequently all out, any
politician with a conscience might have been
expected to suppress the document—or at least
attempt to do so. The idea of calmly putting the
exploded Ananias into circulation as if it! was
something quite good and new looks unique.
Stout, of Maoriland, has a Bill in hand
providing that no person shall have power to will
more than one-third of his or her property away
from the widow or widower and children of the
deceased. This will knock the stuffing out of a
good many of those church bequests, by which the
dying reprobate tries to effect a big fire-insurance
on his soul at the expense of his family. Every-
body has met the huge marble tombstone of that
deceased benefactor who hung on to his money
with teeth and claws, and enjoyed it all his life
and when he was quite done with it piously
sacrificed his family in the good cause, and left
most of the pile to “ charity.” The Bulletin has
always yearned to kick that cheap benefactor.
According to Premier Reid, “ it is not an
argument to say that Protection has been (and is)
the policy of many nations.” It is not an argu-
ment in fa/vor of personal cleanliness to say that
Premier Reid washes himself. Thousands of
people are indisposed to wash themselves, and if
they were all massed together in one nation, their
national policy would be soapless. Yet the fact
of P.R. preferring to keep himself clean is the
best practical proof of his disbelief in dirt that he
can show. And proofs are the nearest approaches
to argument that one can get. Freetraders are
outnumbered by Protectionists to about the same
extent that the absolutely dirt-encrusted people
are outnumbered by cleaner people. This is not
quite an argument, but very near it.
The M.L. Postal Department is being
vigorously canvassed for subscriptions to a Ward
testimonial. The movement was started by some
of the boss officials, so the miserable underling is
made to distinctly understand that his bosses look
favorably on the scheme, which practically means
that they will look unfavorably on the man who
doesn’t support it. Also, Seddon approved of the
movement in Parliament, therefore the vast
shadow of Digger Dick falls on the man who
doesn’t pay up. So the ill-paid postal official is
terrorised and blackmailed into giving his money
and his enthusiastic sympathy to the busted specu-
lator of Invercargill, and Richard of Kumaramakes
another big mistake through not knowing when
to throw his Jonah overboard.
F. C. Gray, M.L.A. for Prahran (Vic.), on
Federation:—
Respecting the question of Federation, I ask the publio
to exercise the greatest care in selecting men of judg-
ment, honor, and ability as its delegates to the forthcom-
ing conference.
Men, in fact, who have not —to use his own
immortal phrase—“ wives in every suburb.” If
the Vic. public has any sense, and such politicians
really exist, it will enthusiastically elect them as
delegates. They are, though sinners, at least men,
with enough red blood in their veins to feed their
brains, whereas politicians of the Gray type are
merely colorless agglomerations of human veal
whose vital force is composed of white corpuscles
and watery serum, out of which nothing good or
bad ever did or could or can come.
The Aveak point of the Yic. Factories Bill
is that it leaves out the laundries worked in con-
nection with “charitable institutions.” The
private steam-laundry must be registered, must
work its employes only certain hours or else pay
overtime, and must have a certificated engine-
driver in charge of its machinery. The charitable
one is exempt, therefore it can work its sweated
employes any hours ; it doesn’t pay overtime—in
fact, it often pays no wages at all, but takes in the
poor and suffering and oppressed in the sacred
name of benevolence, and works them 10 or 12
hours per diem in return for food and clothes and
a hard bed, and much pious exhortation. And for
some wholly unaccountable reason it isn’t com-
pelled to have a qualified engineer, so it may
wreck half-a-street when its mismanaged boiler
goes ker-smash, and scald all the neighborhood
with impunity.
The Yic. Government has produced its
Bill for the regulation of the export-trade. It
provides in the most definite manner possible
that no live-stock, meat, fruit, or dairy produce
shall be exported to any country outside Austra-
lasia unless it has been inspected by a State
official and passed as thoroughly sound. This is
about the roughest thing that ever happened to
the “liberty of the subject,” but it has been found
that the men who send to England damaged pro-
duce of any kind not only get low prices them-
selves but bring down prices all round; one big
consignment of bad butter, for instance; can make
all Victorian butter almost a drug in the market
for weeks afterwards. Therefore, the liberty of
the subject goes under once more in the interests
of the majority. One quaint clause in the Bill
provides that the Agricultural Department may
“declare any spot to be a cool store within the
meaning of the Act.” What’s the matter with
declaring Gehenna to a cool store ?
The life of John Donaldson since 1890 is
the awful moral of what may happen to the
Queenslander who defies M £ ll wraith and the
Q.N. Bank; his death is the point. As Treasurer
to the Morehead Ministry Donaldson brought
down one of the most Democratic budgets seen in
Australia. Its feature was a thumping property-
tax which gave Q.N.B. Manager Drury a fit. All
the overdrafters, and landlords, and mortgagees
started to ring the tocsin, and sulky MTlwraith
came back and gulled the electors, as usual.
Fat-Man took a dire vengeance on his pre-
sumptuous assailant. Donaldson was driven out
of politics and harassed into insolvency ; and
the man who died the other day was only a wreck
of his old self. Latterly, broken in purse and
spirit, he compromised with the enemy, and was
glad to be permitted to take the Logan seat as a
Govt, supporter. Donaldson was an Australian
native, and an honest, straightforward, manly
felloAv whom chicanery found an easy victim.
Crick, M.P., has a comprehensive Bill before
the N.S.W. Assembly, for abolishing the port-
folios of Attorney-General and Postmaster-Gene-
ral ; and for creating a Department of Industry;
and to define, in certain cases, the duties of certain
Ministers. The last idea is so complicated that it
will probably kill the other two. What is wanted
is a measure to make the P.M.G. a permanent,
non-political official, on a purely business basis,
like the Commissioner of Railways ; and to make
the Attorney-General a permanent legal adviser
outside Parliament and the Ministry. Then the
best available lawyer can be appointed, instead of
only the best available lawyer in Parliament on
the Government side of the House, who can spare
time from his private practice—or from his fishing-
excursion. And, being out of Parliament and out
of politics, he will be able to do his duties in a
calm, judicial spirit, which can’t be expected
when Crick and Meagher, and other politicians,
are yelling in his ear and calling him all kinds of
animals.

£lOOO more than Geake’s, and she went to prison

A Melb. District Court case last week sug-
gests what may be the outcome of that which Miss
Watt-Tanner, in “ The Amazons,” calls a season’s
hunting, in the familiar Saturday afternoon waltzes
in the woodlands, by church choirs, varied by

prominent member of a Church of England choir,
got a verdict for the maintenance of her “fine
chubby ” child, by defendant, whilom the church-
organist, a married man.
Two Yankee physicians have devised an
ingenious instrument known as the Eell-O’Dwyer
Artificial Respiration Apparatus, for use in cases
of opium-poisoning. The symptoms after such
poisoning are hard, stertorous respiration, followed
by deep coma, with cyanosis or “blue ing” of the
skin. The apparatus consists practically of an
ordinary spring bellows, worked by the foot, which
drives air in regular spirts into the lungs. In one
bad case it took 2f hours from coma setting in to
counteract its effect. Another opium-case took an
hour. Records prove that, while practically use-
less in coma supervening on cerebral hemorrhage,
it is an almost certain remedy in all cases of
coma due to poisoning by opium preparations.
Such apparatus could be extemporised from an
ordinary kitchen-bellows connected to a rubber
tube, which could be pushed well back into the
mouth.
About the most sensible thing in connec-
tion with the recent Brunswick {Melb.) Orange-
trouble was the remark made by Crown-Prosecutor
Smith, in the subsequent legal proceedings. He
said his father was a Mason, and he had had
enough family experience of the Masonic society
to make him abstain from belonging to any
society holding its meetings in secret. He did
not even join the A.N.A., regarding them as
being as bad as Orangemen. Now the A.N. A. is
after Smith’s scalp, and the Orangemen are up in
arms over the insulting comparison.

remarks.

A crop of libel-actions, as anticipated by
The Bulletin, is about to issue from the Anglo-
Franco-Westralian mining case, heard in Mel-
bourne the other week. Cayron claims £5OOO
damages, for alleged slander, from Dr. Crivelli,
a local sassiety medico ; also, a like amount from
Adolphe Russell, defendant in the mining-suit,
and £lOOO from Jules Borgels, watchmaker.
Meantime, the English syndicate which did such
unsatisfactory business with the Russell faction,
through Cayron aforesaid, is carrying its case
to the Privy Council, and seems certain to starve
the impoverished Frenchmen into capitulation.
At present Russell is clinging to the mine in dis-
pute, without having refunded some £l2OO of
English purchase-money, paid on a contract which
he repudiates. This doesn’t prove a legitimate
sale, but it goes towards supporting a statement
that Judge Hodges, who presided over the case of
the Liberty and Alliance Co.’s v. Russell, failed to
understand it. No outsider could understand it
thoroughly, and a Judge is only a learned out-
sider, after all.

A novel mouse-trap was discovered, the
other day, by a Melb. man, who, when putting
some fresh oysters into his pantry, dropped one on
the floor and omitted to pick it up. Feeling the
air stuffy in the night, the bivalve gaped his shell
for more, whereon three prowling mice inserted
their paws. Oyster gripped them, and kept them
prisoners for the cat.
Dear Bulletin, —Do you think N.S.W.
Govt, could be induced to distribute, among
shearers, drovers, prospectors, travellers, and
others, a few pounds each of imported grass-seeds,
such as rye, prairie-grass, &c. ? Bushmen would
be only too glad to carry and scatter it along the
numerous barren tracks of the interior. — Cooee.
[Dunno ; is grass-seed saleable at a shanty ?
Ed. B.]
An excellent bush-telephone can be made
from the top wire of a fence, good enough to carry
over 25 miles. At straining-posts, a small wire
should be wound closely round the wires on each
side of the post. Gates are overcome by attaching
poles to the gate-posts, and carrying a small wire
across from the fence wires. The mouth and ear
pieces are the usual ones. Of course, in wet
weather the telephone cannot work as well as in a
dry time.
Melb. detective force is arranging a testi-
monial and smoke-night send-off for burly John
Coleman, who retires from the service on his
Eension at the end of the month. Coleman, who
asn’t been a sufficiently prominent thief-catcher
to excite anybody’s jealousy, is popular among the
d’s because he dignifies their calling with his broad
commercial aspect, and can sing a jolly good song.
He’s the plump tenor of the crowd.
Northern Queensland experience seems to
show that the tick is only formidable in coastal
districts. On the Western plains and downs the
exposure to winter-cold and summer-heat seems to
be unfavorable to rapid spread and increase.
Many native birds, particularly the ibis, are very
fond of the insects as food. So rapidly does the
scourge advance southwards that the Bananaland
Parliament needs to revise its tick-legislation every
fortnight.
King O’Malley, of S.A., the wild-eyed
canvasser who has just got into Parliament,
says that the employment of barmaids is “be-
spattering with mud the loving, moon-eyed goddess
of Democracy.” O’Malley’s figures of speech
have a warm fleshiness about them, and a rich,
fruity, soulful hugginess, and a poetic sentiment
of “ the altogether.” Even when discussing the
finances of a road-board his language is that of a
man who is embracing a lovely woman ap lie talks,
and is troubled through her ambrosial fringe
getting into his mouth.
Editor Bulletin, —Apropos your recent
par. on Melb. medical-clubs, let me, as one who
knows the subject, enlighten the public by a few
facts from actual experience. To begin with, al-
though (id. weekly for attendance and medicine is
low enough for anything, the average rate paid
for attendance only by the friendly societies is
only 3d. weekly, and their cost for medicine, it
may be safely said, does not exceed Id. weekly.
Even allowing for cost of canvassing and collect-
ing, this leaves a small margin in favor of the
private club. True, again, that the latter will
take the man at =£s weekly and the navvy at ss. a
day ; but it will not, like the friendly society, take
the well-to-do publican or lawyer or estate-agent
or storekeeper or the retired squatter or the civil-
servant at <£looo a year, members of all of which
classes (and I speak by the card) are -bene-
fiting greatly by the 3d.-a-week arrangement.
Eurther, the arrogance, the injustice, and
the utter want of consideration shown by
most friendly societies towards their medical
officers have driven many of them to seek
any loophole of escape from what is, in many
cases, an intolerable position. The opinions uni-
versally expressed as to friendly societies by
medicos, when only medicos are present, do not
vary throughout Australia. (In Melbourne they
are only a little more violent than usual.) The
private club has this further advantage, that the
subscriber can, in cases of incompetence or neglect,
change his doctor without forfeiting any other
privileges; while the doctor can eject an
obnoxious member without quarrelling with
a powerful and generally spiteful secret
society. As to the allegation of incapacity
to do the work properly for the sum mentioned, I
find that, for a busy month, the average number
of patients seen is at the rate of six a day for every
100 members that is, a lodge numbering 1000
members, and bringing in a fair income, would
involve seeing 60 patients a day. Fair work, but
nothing overwhelming, for it must be remembered
that the majority of cases are quite trivial—in fact,
many men in good practice daily see twice that
number. The medico with the private lodge
can only slouch his work or be ignorant of it at
the expense of losing it altogether—members are
not easy to get and very easy to lose ; and he can-
not square any friendly Nob’e Grand or arch
Buffalo or boss Forester to keep the rest of the
flock in order. It is pretty cutthroat work in
Melb., but it is certainly a case of the survival of
the fittest. Apart from all this, the private medi-
cal lodge is doing good work, for there are thou-
sands in this destitute city who cannot afford
more than 6d. a week (the friendly society’s total
subscription is from Is. 3d. to Is. 6d.), and there
are thousands more (single women and men of a
certain age) who are not , eligible for friendly
societies. —G.D.

Dear Bulletin, —Be the “ gibba ” quartz-
crystal found in an aboriginal “ bora ” cemetery.
During a flood on the Macleay River a stray abo-
riginal youth, making his way to the main camp
situated on the high land, swam the river naked
and was found by a friend of mine dangling from
a gum-stump by his toes —quite dead. He had
on him a tin match-box in vogue in the sixties ; it
measured about Gin. long, and inside it was a
smaller one which, in its turn, enclosed another.
The crystal, about the size of a pumpkin-seed,
was contained in this last and wrapped in rag.
The blacks came when the flood had subsided, and
searched closely ; also inquired for the talisman ;
but my friend, was afraid to give it up, for if a
woman keeps this “mundi mundi,” as it is called,
she is killed with the tomahawk. By the way, in
the Awaba dialect of Port Macquarie “ bora
ground is “capera” ground, the place of rites,
burial, and battle.— Dulcangi.
Over two cartloads of mussels were re-
moved from the bottom of a barque docked at
Wellington (M.L.) recently. Which recalls the
fact that mussels were unknown in Port -rlump
until the docking or beaching of an English vessel
at Sandridge in the early days. The molluscs, as
they were scraped off the vessel, dropped into the
bay, and became the progenitors of the countless
tons of mussels which rot, during summer, upon
the foreshores of St Kilda and Albert Park.
