SPORTING NOTIONS.
“ Monico ” writes to The Bulletin from Lon-
don :—“The collapse against the M.C.C. and Ground
was too overwhelming to be painful at the time, and
Darling’s dour determined stand next day was badly
wanted to save Australians’ prestige for pluck from
obliteration. The Adelaide man, with his left leg behind
his bat to stop breaking balls he missed, refused to he
hurried by wicket-eager bowers. Over and over again
the trundier had to turn back in his preliminary whilst
the batsman calmly mopped his brow, carefully took up
his position, and grimly tried to avert the one-innings
defeat. And the crowd was with him, too. Applause
follow d every score, and his reception at the close
almost approached an ovation. Had Giffen but gone in
to keep up one end—if a man can lounge about the
wickets he can stand at the wickets—the result might
have been slightly different. At present the less said
about Giff. and other matters the better. The opinion of
Bill Murdoch, whose persuasiveness availed not, would be
valuable—and vigorous.
“For the first time in Ehgland Eady batted
with confidence—after the opening overs—and on an im-
proving wicket, against J. T. Ilearne, Pougher, Davidson,
Grace, and Jackson, made his highest score up to date.
The Tasmanian is a raw colt, and after placing a ball in
the long-slips stood half-way up the pitch to see if it were
fielded. ‘ Run ! ’ roared Darling ; and the giant got home
and spread himself in a broad, boyish smile. When the
Englishmen were batting-, ‘ long-off ’ was missing at the
opening of an over, and discovered, still at ‘ deep square-
leg,’ absorbed in thoughts of Raspberry Land and hitting
Windsor for ‘ sixes.’
“Gregory, with an average of 41, is batting
pluckily and consistently ; Darling and Trott with not too
much luck ; Giffen, Donnan, and Hill should do better ;
Graham throws his wicket away; Iredale seldom has it to
throw away ; Eady rapidly improving ; Trumble surpris-
ing the scorners; Kelly doing doggedly; and Jones,
Johns, and M Tib bin not attempting to upset expecta-
tions.”
* * * *
An Australian abroad gossips to The Bulle-
tin : —“ Old IC. M. Grace, now 52, has rarely played county
cricket of late, and it was only as a last resort'that he was
included for Gloucester against the Australians. The
doctor (for, like ‘ W.G.,’ he practises medicine in his spare
moments) still plays a lot of cricket, however, as captain
of the local club, and there he rules the roast. On one
occasion he was given ‘out’ l.b.w. by the umpire. He
protested : ‘ Nonsense, I was not out.’ 1 No,’ replied the
umpire, ‘not out, the doctor knows best’ —and E.M.
stopped where he was.
“ It will be a pity if such matches as the Aus-
tralian XI. have played at Wembley Park and the Crystal
Palace are allowed to find a place on every programme.
‘ An Eleven of England’ is such a vague, unrepresenta-
tive combination, as a rule, that nobody cares how the
result goes, and the games are only arranged to give
amusement-managers a chance of a cut in at a good spec.
The fact that both of the above-named matches must
have meant a loss to the speculators who gave the Aus-
tralians a £250 guarantee, is not to be deplored.
“ The Lord’s crowd howled with delight and
derision as the Australians went down one after the other
before the M.C.C. In comparison with those howls the
dead silence amidst which the previous victories had been
won seemed, on reflection, almost abject.
“ Commenting on the opinions of the press that
‘ funk ’ was responsible for the score of 18 Australia made
against M.C.C., Trumble remarked, ‘ All I have to say is
what Bonnor said years ago under similar circumstances :
* The ball that bowled me would have bowled the Creator,
and there was no funk.’
“The stand made by Darling and Eady in the
Australians’ second innings against the M.C.C. was most
interesting, as with Giffen ill and M'Kibbin only to come
in, it was the last chance of saving an ignominious defeat.
At the same time, the play was of the slowest. During
40min. only two runs were made, and Eady did not break
his duck until he had been at the wicket over half-an
hour. Before each ball he received Darling would take
an extraordinary time bracing himself up, patting the
turf, and finally settling himself to bat.
“ To be dependent on Eady to save a match is a
most anxious time, for he swings his bat in such wild
fashion before each stroke that it looks anv odds against
his eventually getting on to the ball. You never feel he’s
really set.
“J. T. Hearne, who bowled so well for the
M.C.C. against Australia and got all the wickets in their
second innings, is undoubtedly, on his present form, about
the best bowler in England. Pougher, on the other hand,
who did such sensational work in the same match, though
a good bowler is not great, and would certainly not be in-
cluded in an All-England eleven unless great weight is
given to his performance on this particular occasion.
Richardson is very in-and-out, and is either very effective
or very expensive, according to the ground.
“ Taken as a whole, the cricket-grounds of
England are badly-managed. The catering is almost in-
variably of the worst description. The scoring-boards are
most primitive, even at Lord’s. The public are allowed,
nearly everywhere, to encroach upon the playing-ground
—sometimes to the extent of 20yds., and invariably four
or five, and at some places the batsmen have to push their
way through the crowd as they go to and from the
wickets.
“ Few Australian cricketers would know Iredale
for the same man if they saw him batting in England and
had no previous experience of his play but the brilliance
of his performances in his own country. It is only now
and again that one of his strokes even resembles Sydney
form. At the beginning of the tour he was excused on
the ground of strangeness to English wickets ; now it
seems to be faint-heartedness only.”
But there has been no faint-heartedness about
Iredale’s latest innings.
* * * *
“The Australians, who batted first, began
badly,” and “The Australians went first to the wickets”
(Argus and Age reports of the M.O.C. match). If this
had been Trott’s seventh successive time of losing the
toss, instead of winning it, the point would have ob-
truded itself considerably. As for example : “ The extra-
ordinary bad luck of the Australians still pursued them,
and the choice of innings fell to their opponents, as
usual.” Up to then Trott’s two-headed bob, or whatever
the talisman may be, had taken 18 tosses out of 22. He
started the tour with a sequence of six wins, against
which the odds were 50 to 1, or thereabouts, and has
beaten that record since.
* * * *
Strange that the Australians should make a
disastrous beginning in each of their first three matches
at Lord’s. They must have suffered from over-anxiety to
astonish the cockneys.
* * * *
Hulme, the Derbyshire bowler, was no more
good against the Australians when, the novelty had worn
off his attack. He took their wickets freely the first time
they met him ; next time he didn’t trouble them much ;
the third time, which was last week, he was seemingly as
easy as falling off a log.
* * * *
A Cockney cricket-reporter dubbed Iredale “the
Australian Don Quixote.”
• * * *
M.C.C. and Ground means the “amateur”
members of the Marylebone Club, plus sundry profes-
sionals who get a retaining-fee—practically everybody of
first-class cricket importance, with the exception of pros,
attached to the rival Surrey Club.
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin,— Why not .call the last test-
match, not England v. Australia, but “ Australia v. Europe
and Asia”? I am quite sure that were Quong Tart or
Kong Meng possessed of G. Giffen’s abilities they would
not be included in an Australian Eleven.— Haliva.
* * * *
The Age did not post up the final result of the
second test-match on Sunday ; but the Argus was honest
enough to admit that it works on Sunday, and stuck up
the full scores as soon as they came through, and, more-
over, left them up all the evening for the church-goers to
see.
* * * *
It is not impossible that Giffen will, after all,
come out with the best Australian batting average.
Gregory, Iredale, and Qo. have meteoric flashes, but
George pegs along all the time, never going below fourth
on the list. He has not come with his big score yet.
* * * *
M.C.C. was deprived of a victory by the play-
for-time policy of Trott and Co. ; still the Australians are
entitled to the credit of achieving 331 runs in their
second innings. They made a fair bid for a “ draw,” and
got it, as they did at Sheffield Park in their first match.
But showing the difference between the two schools of
batting—on the second day M.C.C. scored 181 before
lunch, and on the third day the Australians stuck at the
wickets until five o’clock for 218. In fact, they had alto-
gether about twice as much time at the wickets as the
Englishmen were able to get, and only made 68 more
runs. C’est la guerre, mats ce n’est pas magnifique.
Iredale and Giffen together may be credited with
about 50 per cent, of the Australian victory in several
recent matches.
» * * *
The first turn days’ play in a Sussex v. Somerset
match produced 924 runs.
* * * - *
The selection of the nest All-England Eleven
will, no doubt, be considerably influenced by the form
which M.C.C. and Surrey professionals have shown against
the Australians. Anyway, M‘Laren, always a patchy per-
former, and Briggs, if not Stoddart or Jackson likewise,
may have to stand aside in favor of three bowlers who can
bat a bit, whilst Pougher may take the place of J. T.
Hearne. Peel is a probable choice on account of the
veteran left-hander recovering some of his old skill with
the ball. But it seems impossible for All-England to raise
a formidable bowling team that shall not have a weak
batting tail. And the main hope of any selection would
be Richardson. If that important item happens to be out
of sorts, the Britishers-cum-Ranji will be shorn of half
their strength.
* * * *
In age and weight, the All-England lot can still
do the Australians, although the young and scraggy
Indian Prince detracts from their supremacy in both par-
ticulars.
* * * *
Re the third and final test-match. With a good
wicket to start upon the All-England eleven are verj r
likely to win easily if they get first whack at the ball,
whereas the Australians are not likely to have much to
spare if they win the toss, unless the' weather works in
their favor. With a bad wicket throughout the match
the Australians arc very likely to win, whichever way the
luck of the toss may go. No Australian team has been
beaten by All-England in bowlers’ weather, nor had any
A.E. combination been licked on their own ground in
batsmen’s weather, until the other Saturday. And then
they weren’t licked much. Only two of them were clean
bowled, as against five Australians. Two of them (in-
cluding the “crack,” Jackson) got run out, whereas none
of our men threw away their chance of scoring. Poor old
Grace fell over in attempting to swipe one of Trott’s
slows, and was ignominious; y stumped. The awful failure
of all their bowlers, bar Richardson, put them almost
beyond the possibility of winning. They batted rashly,
had bad luck, yet they were only beaten by three wickets.
For the Australians, it is contended that Jones wasn’t
able to bowl his best, but considering the form shown by
M'Kibbin it seems doubtful whether Jones’ breakdown
wasn’t a gain instead of a loss. They can’t all be bowling
at once. M'Kibbin was picked as Jones’ understudy,
and when called upon to act in place of the Adelaide man
he did exceptionally well.
* * * *
Dear B.—The Bulletin, strangely enough, is
the only Australian paper I know that displays a British
bias in its comments on international cricket. It has an
astonishing weakness for explaining away Australian
victories, and pointing out how different it might have
been if something had happened that neglected to occur,
or if somebody were on the job who was dead or unavoid-
ably detained. For this reason The Bulletin should be
interested in my three Englishmen. The first was a tram-
conductor with a Yorkshire flavor. On the Sunday on
which the finals of the second test-match came through
I asked him for the very latest. “ The Englishmen have
lost it,” he said. “ They can win when they like, you
know, but they must have had a bit on.” The other true
Britons I met during the afternoon, and each assured me
on his honor as a gentleman that the All-England Eleven
had sold the match. Here is patriotism in its noblest
form! The Britisher says: “Think us rogues if
you like, but don’t imagine for a moment that you can
beat us at cricket.” For my part I know that the three
matches the present Australian Eleven have lost were
sold in the open market to the highest bidder.—Yours
Australian Blow.
* * * *
What with ten falls, the death of Dungan, in-
juries to jockeys, and the trip-up of favorites, there was
no end of sport at Moonee Valley Saturday. Waterloo
was easily beaten in the steeple. The longer-distanced
Caulfield ’chase may suit him better. Stable-mate Dun-
gan won his first race on the course on which he broke
his neck.
* ♦ * *
From Melb. Age’s account of Galway’s sensa-
tional win in the Caulfield Steeple : “ Although the race
was yet far from over, the spectators cheered to the echo
hats went up, and in the vice-regal box Lord Brassey and
Lady Brassey were observed to be completely carried
away by the excitement of the situation.” The man who
could turn away from such a race to contemplate Gov.
Brassey’s countenance is herewith awarded first prize for
grovel.
* * » *
Galway’s extraordinary Caulfield Steeplechase
recovery recalls F. D. Brewer’s performance on Comet at
Epsom, some years ago. Comet fell, was remounted and
won, though handicapped by broken stirrups ’ But
Comets field was mostly dead, while at least three of
Galway’s opponents were genuinely backed.
Apropos, Blue Mountain baulked at the first
fence, and won Caulfield G.N. Steeple. At Flemington,
w-, 1 , 0 , f ?u oom - an and then beat a field of three
Will-o -the-Wisp won the Hunt Club Cup after falling.
Godfrey Watson remounted Busaco thrice in a Grand
National, but just lost.

A subdued-looking feminine follower of form
smilingly asked a certain owner the other day for a tip
“ for the next.” Disremembering a previous meeting and
introduction, he savagely told her to “ go yonder and ask
the secretary for information ; ha was running the show.”
The subdued one happened to be the secretarv’s wife.
* * * *
Tire is the only horse left in Melb. Cup who
carries exact w.f.a. Wallace has 141 b. more than w.f.a.,
and one very minor hummer carries 401 b. less.
* * * *
Geldings have a three pounds advantage in
A.J.C. Derby, as usual, this year; but in the V.R.C
masculine and neuter gender meet on equal terms.
* * * *
A.J.C. Epsom and Metropolitan weights dis-
close but little Spring information. Both are run at a
time when owners don’t know whether to “wait” or
“ spin.” The pick of the Epsom would appear to be Pro-
jectile (if well), and Toreador for the Metrop. Trentham
and Nada may represent St. Alban’s instead of Wallace.
The prad of one very smart crowd, waiting for months,
has been given 71b. more than expected in the mile
flutter.
* * * *
Planting a fine silken sponge in a horse’s nostril
is the latest ’Murkan method of running a cronk ’un. It
retards breathing.
* * * *
Gov. Gerard Smith’s purchase of baby racers
means a Govt. House livery in the near future. His Ex.
will be the only vice-regal owner in the Australias. Quality
isn’t a characteristic of W.A. neddies, and a good horse is
a little bank if his training ex.’s don’t run into more than
the stakes.
* * * *
M.L. anti-tote speeches were merely echoes of
former mumblings on the same subject. They would
have passed for “ Essays on Mechanical Gambling, by
Ikey Mo,” the twang of the ring heralding them all.
“Jimmy” Carroll was the only Cabinet Minister to bar-
rack for the tote as against the walking “ take-downs.”
t There are no last-minute scratchings allowed up
North Q. way. All big-event candidates must be elimin-
ated 24 hours before. And if there is any suspicion of
hanky-panky stewards disqualify owners. The system
works well. Yet Melb. and Sydney authorities aver that
a late-scratching rule is out of' the question.

* * * *
All Townsville cheerfully plunged on Cabin Boy
for the local Cup when the weights appeared, and the
nng gave credit freely after mopping in available cash.
People didn’t seem to notice the elasticity of their books
until the favorite was scratched. The incident was
responsible for a big rumpus and many accusations.
Meanwhile, Ikey Mo is living at the best pub.

“The New Woman has bad legs, is retiring
from the turf, and going to the country.”—Melb. Age
sport item.
* ♦ * *
Cricketer Trumble, a keen racing man, writes
that English judges can’t stand Daimio at any price. He
also mentions that Paris looks like breaking down. Bad
legs.
* * * *
The travelling Q. team walked over Sydney’s
Rugby corpse in Saturday’s interprovincial leather-argu-
ment. The visitors were too fast and clever for the locals
and there was no luck about their 18 to 7 win. Short
passes were a prominent feature, and the occasional
monopoly of the ball in this respect really decided the
game. Also, the Q. backs were stubborn and sure, and
wing-forwards were discarded. This gave an additional
man to the scrum. Sydney had “ wings,” but they were
promptly clipped every time.
* * * *
The N.S.W. team has been considerably revised
for the- second battle with Q. A village-green crowd
would have shaped better than they did in the initial
match. The tourists have six men to fall back on, and
intend putting their best team in next Saturday.
* * * »
Q. skipper Cockcroft played first-class Rugby in
M.L. for 15 years, and owns a big forward reputation
“Cocky” has taught his men all the M.L. tricks, and also-
a few little things of his own. They ought to score at
every point in Maoriland, bar perhaps Taranaki. The un-
assuming crowd there have been more or less on top ever
since they showed Stoddart’s Englishers how to kick some
years back.
* * * *
Saturday’s Melb. football-matches, so far the
most interesting of the season, left the Premiership posi-
tions undisturbed. Essendon’s lOg. to 3g. against Foots-
cray were mainly got by systematic forward rushes.
Croft, a junior recruit, was responsible for three goals
besides a fine game. Collingwood and S. Melb. each woni
their matches. Melb. beat Carlton 7 to 3, and Fitzroy
Port Melb. Sensational St. Kilda played another brilliant,
game against Geelong, winning for the fifth year in suc-
cession by 7g. to sg. August will probably decide the
Premiership, as the leading clubs, Essendon, Collingwood
South Melb., Melb., and Fitzroy, meet one another. ’
* * * *
The case of umpire-stoushing at North Mel-
bourne last Saturday is another nail in the coffin of Vic-
torian football, regarded as a game for respectable patron-
age. Blackguardism grows more demonstrative as the
general public begins to stay away, and in accordance
with the eternal fitness of things the rorty Collingwood
team will probably win the blanky futball championship
for the blanky season. Let them win and wear it ever-
more.
» * * *
Gov. Brassey—or was it the N.S.W. potentate ?
—must be chuckling rather sadly. One of them remarked
the other day, that a man of extraordinary courage was
needed for the billet of umpire at a Melbourne football-
match. One little decision which may happen to turn the
scale in favor of Collingwood versus “Noreth,” or
“ Sowth,” or the other way, is a terrible thing for an un-
armed person to deal with.
* * •
Huret, the French champion cyclist, who lately
defeated Johnson, the Yankee crack, threatens to visit
Australia next season. If the flock of foreigners who
announce their intention of taking Australian tracks
prove as successful as plugger Bilmartin and ’Arris, the
home-made cyclists will soon have to agitate for a duty
on the imported article, or discover some handicappers
with a less delicate sense of hospitality.
Apropos of France : There has just been intro-
duced into Australia a French chainless cyole claimed to
be “ perfect.”
* * » *
George Turner (eldest son of Premier Turner),
the Melb. amateur who carried off the Three-Mile Handi-
cap from scratch in Brisbane, is a 19-year old, 9st.
youngster of thin build, and, like the great Zimmy, is an
astounding “ take-down.” Seen for the first time Turner’s
clumsy way of working his machine causes the spectators
to laugh at him until he gets his calfless limbs into play.
Then he does travel; possibly, like a few other men,.
Turner’s calves are on his heart instead.

The few effects of the late Henry Parkes,
who died intestate, have been taken possession of
by the Curator, who will have them auctioned on
an early date. The old man’s sole assets consist
of some books, pictures, carvings, &c.

H. Hayes, riding first in the recent big N.S.W.
road cycle-race, won a “ performance ” vvhose hum shou.d
reach the bounds of the Australian cycling world. Also,
the Humber cycle notched a win which will give it
another peg on which to hang sold Humbers. The race’s
first prize was a Humber, given by the Humber people,
and Haves, the winner, rodoa Humber, with Dunlop tires.
* * * *
The absurdity of the League of Yie. Wheelmen
officials in choosing the Coburg to Somerton course in-
stead of a decent stretch of road, for the decision of the
25-Mile Road Premiership, last Saturday, did not prevent
A. A. Middleton from pedalling over its bumpy surface in
1 hour 16min. 36sec. This really magnificent perform-
ance will not directly benefit the plucky wobbler one iota
individually, as the prizes are awarded only to the clubs
on points, and Middy’s E. Melb. mates were too far behind
to score nearer than a second place to S. Melb.
* * * »
The Interprovincial Cycling Conference sitting
in Melbourne has given a nasty slap to Melb. Bicycle Club,
whence all the Vic. delegates are drawn, by ruling that at
all League-patronised meetings the entrance and accept-
ance fees of any race shall not exceed 1 per cent, of the
prize-money, the M.B.C. has for years been paying the
whole big prize-money of its much-boomed Austral Wheel
Race (£250) out of its entrance and acceptance fees, which
totalled £2 per man.
During the past couple of seasons, cycling has
been made farcical by every tenth-rate back-block town-
ship running a “ one-mile champidnship of Australia,”
With £l, 10s., and ss. prizes, at its annual sports-meeting
in ptlWlican Dooley's paddock. Henceforth, only one
championship per year is to be allowed, each province to
have the different distances in rotation, and prizes of not
less than £lO and £5 are to be offered. A suggestion that
only bona fide residents of Australia be allowed to com-
pete was fired out—in the interests of “ gate ”
Some enthusiastic Victorians are discussing the
probable result of a match between their idol Parsons,
and Linton, the English crack. Martin, who is not in the
same class as Linton, showed himself to be Parsons’ equal
at any distance, so that if the match eventuates the
swelled heads of Parsons’ admirers will collapse with a
bang.
* * * *
Marschner, the trick-rider, the other day rode
his machine over the top of a Melb. factory in order to
demonstrate the many advantages of a local two-speed
gear. The invention is simple and works well enough,
but is never likely to pass beyond the fad-stage, as the
tourist is not in a hurry up a hill, and the margin between
the low and high gears is not sufficient to be of service to
the racing-man.
* * » *
Cyclist Martin has bagged coin in Q. At one
place he won five out of six events, and at another a £25
25-mile race, beating each of five riders at the end of every
five-iniles, was a gift for him. The ’Murkan wants to re-
present Brisbane at Sydney League’s meet next month.
* * * *
Melb. demise: Octogenarian boat-builder (Green-
land. The three good-humored rivals at the Prince’s
Bridge sheds have been Edwards, Greenland, and Fuller,
though the first ran away from the other two with the
business. Greenland was the Yarra Dictionary, “The
Man in the Ditch,” the oracular Capt. Jack Bunsby of the
concern. When he put in his oar on the rowing question
criticism was dumb.
* * * *
Pretty safe to say that the Murkan scullers
have been practising ever since Stanbury was announced
for the Thames. Had he gone under they would have
badgered him right and left. But that record paddle has
made them ill.
* * * *
Sporting journalism seems to confuse the
brothers Gibson with the brothers Manifold. It has been
more than once misstated that Dungan and Dairnio came
from the same stable.
* * • *
The extra meetings initiated by the Y.R.C. last
year showed a bare profit of £42 all told. Oup-week
yielded close on £lO,OOO, and expenses —working and
general—were reduced by £5OOO. The club is in a
thoroughly healthy condition again.
* * * *
Cycle shops are becoming as common as tobac-
conists’, but, so far, there has been little cutting of prices.
When it does come, what a smash there vill be !
* * * *
The nice discrimination made between town and
rural horses by city stewards has become most marked.
In any cases of disqualification for oronkness the country
prad generally secures the holiday.

THE AUSTRALIAN HEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Naturalist Milligan, of Traralgon, Vic.,
avers that the white-eyed crow has “ loyal ” brown
eyes when a fledgling, which become china-white
about a year after hatching. Though often called
the “ carrion crow,” he won’t feed on carrion. In
captivity he wall take fresh meat, but is strongest
on garden fruit and pepper-tree berries, especially
in summer. Round about Corner Inlet, flocks of
white-eyed crows chum up with sandpipers, sea-
curlews and oystercatchers, and feed with them on
the marine life left by the tide in sand-shallows
and mud-flats.
A quaint example of the disjointedness of
this great h’Empire appears in a divorce-case on
which the English Courts are now trying their
teeth with small results. That is to say, the
results aren’t much so far as the case is concerned,
but the floor is covered with teeth, and the law is
next going to tackle the problem with its gums.
The case is something like this : One Dunster got
married in England, and afterwards settled with
his wife in N.S.W. He was divorced there, the
separated wife going to England, while Dunster
moved on to Maoriland. He was married again
in that province, and later on he and his
wife went back to England, where they ran
upagainst the old wife. Then the trouble
began. The old wife sued for divorce on the
ground that Dunster had deserted her and com-
mitted bigamy, it being alleged on her behalf that
the N.S.W. divorce is of no account, being only a
common colonial separation, and that she is still
married to Dunster and wants to get rid of him.
Dunster opposes, on the ground that he and the
first Mrs. D. are already separated and can’t be
separated any more ; if he doesn’t maintain this,
and the Court only divorces the first Mrs. Dunster
now, it follows, of course., that the second Mrs.
Dunster has not been married until now, which is
an awkward situation. And the English Divorce
Court professes not to know whether a N.S.W.
divorce can dissolve an English marriage, that
being a superior kind of marriage which can’t be
lightly interfered with by any “ blawsted kaw-
liny.” At recent advices the case was adjourned
indefinitely to allow the parties time to pour
water over each other to cool their heated brains.

THE AGED.
Extracts from the “ true poem of aN.S.
Wales lunatic-asylum keeper ” received by The
Bulletin
I am a Lunatic Asylum Keeper
In this land, so beautiful and free,
And I don’t suppose a deeper,
Harder, soul-destroying occupation there could be.
Every day, from morn to night,
Lunatics I have to dress and feed ;
And oft before the morning’s light
Round Bedlam’s walls I have to speed.
I must the worst abuse repel
With nought but words like honey sweet,
For else the doctors will expel
My humble self into the dusty street.
Severe attacks I have to stand
From raving madmen, fearless, strong and bold,
Who bite, and strike with hand
As hard as Cromwell’s Ironsides of old.
At night, when other people sleep,
I have few hours that are still;
Some maniac will cry and weep
All night, until I feel quite ill.
All this I have to bear
For fear of losing place and pay ;
And, if I drop a tear,
Remember: I get shillings four per day.
I shall, however, pay them back;
Some day I really mad will be,
And then i will them whack
Until the rank injustice they can see.


An old-hand’s justice. Year 1869. Scene,
a N.S.W. border town. Billy Maloney, late of
Hobart Town, has been on the spree, but had re-
plenished his swag before starting the orgy.
Sobering up, he asks Paunchy, the publican, tor
h s bundle. This is not Billy’s first failure in
Borrowaway, so Paunchy tries to ‘ have him.
“Why, you sold it to me for fourteen bob!”
“ You villain, I’ll have the value !”- —and he put
his fists through all the bottom windows of the
Royal (God save the name !). When ascending to
complete the decoration of the top storey, the law
reached out its long arm. “Three months, but
you must get your swag back : there was no sale.
After fourteen weeks, Billy again breasts the
bar : “Paunchy, I’m here to smash more windows.
A clay’s free drink and a solatium of five shillings
induce him to migrate across the river into Vic-
toria, muttering as he goes, “A crown sd- -cl
little for three months.” Oh ! for the good old
times and timers.— Scotty the Wrinkles.

THIS Company is being formed for the purpose of acquiring the property known as “ HANNAN S
HILL EXTENDED,” at Kalgoorlie, being Lease No. 2688 E, containing 15 acres.
Reports by Robert Gibson, Esq. (the well-known Mining Engineer, representing the Octagon and
other Syndicates), and Brian Hooker, Esq. (late Manager of the Ivanhoe G.M. Co.), are appended,
and give full particulars of work done and the future prospects of the property. .
It is estimated that the amount of capital to be placed to the credit of the Company will be
sufficient to open up the property and erect plant and machinery. ,
It is proposed to apply to the Stock Exchanges of London, Sydney, Melbourne, Adelaide, Berth,
Coolgardie, Kalgoorlie, and Menzies, to have the Company registered on their Call Cists.
The Company will be considered formed when 30,000 shares are applied for.
Upon the formation of the Company, a meeting of allottees of shares will be held in
pursuant to Clause 17 of the Companies Act, 1892, to elect Directors and adopt Memorandum and
Articles of Association. .
The following Agreements have been entered into, and may be inspected at the offices ot Messrs.
Jenkins & Abbott, Solicitors, Bayley-street, Coolgardie .
Between E. G. S. Hare and L. Levinson of the one part, and AML Jenkins of the other
part, for the sale and purchase of Gold Mining Lease 2688 E, dated 6th June, 1896.
Between A. G. Jenkins of the one part and Murdo Scobie Mackenzie as Trustee for the
Company, of the other part, for the sale and purchase of Gold Mining lease 2688 E,
dated 16th June, 1896.
Dated at Coolgardie, June 18th, 1896.
Report by ROBERT GIBSON, Esq., M.E., Consulting Engineer to the Octagon Syndicate.
To the Promoters of the Kalgoorlie Prince of Wales Mine.
At your request I have made a thorough inspection of Gold Mining Lease No. 2688 E, known as
“Hannan’s Hill End Extended,” and beg to hand you herewith my report on same.
This property comprises a total area of 15 acres, being surveyed portion No. 2GBBE. It is
splendidly situated, adjoining as it does the famous “ Hannan’s Reward ’’ on the east, the Hannan s
Proprietary Co.’s property on the west, whilst to the north lies the Hannan s Reward ISorth and
“ Hannan’s Central ” Gold Mines. I look upon this section as one of the best position blocks available
at the present time in this district. On the Hannan’s Reward property they have proved the lode
down to a depth of 300 feet in hard country, and at that level the lode has an assay value of 2oz. gold
per ton, over a width of 5 feet. , .
Towards the centre of the section is a small hill, and along the cap of this rise is the outcrop of an
immense body of quartzite, quartz, and ironstone mixed, which varies m width up to 20 feet The
trend of this outcrop is almost due north and south, and underlying east On the top of the hill, and
on the west side of the outcrop, a shaft has been sunk to a depth of 100 feet, and a crosscut driven east
to cut the lode formation. At a point 100 feet from the shaft the first indications of the lode were met
with the crosscut passing into an ironstone formation, intermixed throughout with quartz seams. This
has been driven on for a short distance, but, in my opinion, it is merely an offshoot from the mam body
of stoned At 128 feet from the shaft the main lode was struck. The crosscut is now 5 feet into the
lode and no walls are showing. It is a fine body of stone, and has a much more pleasing appearance
than on the surfaeeyeontaining as it does a much larger percentage of iron in the form of hematite, and
bands of quartz intermixed throughout the quartzite. .
The whole of this lode gives very encouraging prospects, and it is a promising indication of better
results being obtained when further developed. _ .
Further north along this outcrop another shaft has been sunk to a depth of 80 feet, and a crosscut
driven east, but this will have to be extended a considerable distance further to cut the reef as it is
dipping eastward. A gold-bearing quartz leader is also showing m the west side of this shaft foi a
width of 6 to 8 inches.
Towards the RE. corner a costeen exposes a strong ironstone formation for a width of 2 feot. This
has been proved gold bearing, and is well worthy further development. It has a trend IN. ~ an
probably junction with the main lode within this section. ... . ,
In addition to the visible outcrops on this area the surface indications point favorably to the
existence of other gold-bearing formations.
The developments on the surrounding properties are more than sufficient to warrant a vigorous
policy of prospecting being carried out. The position of this section leaves nothing to be desire ,
I have no hesitation in strongly recommending it as a mining venture of undoubted value.
I am, dear sirs, yours faithfully,
Robert Gibson, M.E.,
(Consulting Engineer to the Octagon Syndicate).
Report by BRIAN HOOKER, Esq., A R.S.M., Lond., Late Mining Manager for
Ivanhoe Gold Mine.
To the Promoters of the Kalgoorlie Prince of Wales Mine.
Acting under your instructions, I have inspected Lease No. 2688 E, known as Hannan s Hill
Extended.
The Lease, containing an area of 15 acres, is situated about a-third of a mile east of Kalgoorlie
township and is bounded on the west by Hannan’s Reward Gold Mining Co., on the north by
Hannan’s Reward North Gold Mining Co., on the east by Hannan’s proprietary Development Co.,
and on the south by the Colonial Goldfields, Ltd.
On the top of the hill, in the centre of the Lease, is a bold outcrop of quartz and ironstone, striking
north and dipping east, which can be traced for a long distance through the property ; this lode carries
gold, its thickness has not been determined, but it is without doubt large.
There are two shafts sunk to test this lode at a depth, each of which is 100 feet deep.
In the north shaft, at the bottom, is a reef about a foot thick, striking north, dipping west; but no
development work has been done on it. A crosscut has been driven to the east, 80 feet in length, but
the lode has not been reached as yet, though there are indications that it is not far off.
The most important development is in the south shaft, from the bottom of which a crosscut has
been driven to the east 128 feet long. At a distance of 100 feet a lode formation 4 feet thick, striking
north, dipping east, has been passed through, and has also been driven on to the south 12 feet—it is oi
the same character as the usual Hannan’s lodes. At the end of the crosscut another formation has been
struck and penetrated for about 4 feet without reaching the hanging-wall ; it is composed of silicious
ironstone, and carries visible gold, at times coarse. It is impossible to give its thickness as yet, nor yet
its strike, 5 but it is dipping to the east. It is, in fact, premature to give as yet a decided opinion as to
its value,'but I think that when the hanging-wall is reached it will be found to be of even greater value
than it is at present.
In the north-east corner of the lease another lode, about 2 feet thick, has been exposed by means of
a trench, from which lode a prospect can be obtained by dollying.
The lease derives considerable value from its position alone, apart from the fact that a lode has
been proved to exist to a depth of 1.00 feet, so, from my knowledge of the Hannan’s district, I fee’
myself justified in recommending the property as one .well worthy of development.
Yours faithfully,
Brian Hooker,
(Associate of the Royal School of Mines, London ; late Queensland Government Mineralogist;

Dear Bulletin,—l saw it in the window

Dear Bulletin,— Re a recent par. of yours.
If only Randolph Bedford would go seriously for
the G«vernor-generalship of Australia, what a
time the country would have ! “ What a time .
Whatatime ! What a glaring time!” — J’Aiderais.
“Hyde Pank, London,” writes an Austra-
lian abroad to The Bulletin, “ was almost gal-
vanised out of its rigid vacuousness one recent
morning by the appearance of a lovely girl on a
pink-enamelled bike, followed by a flunkey m
groom’s garb, also on two wheels. The maiden
was innocently young, and her long, loose hair
quivered in golden undulations to her shapely
waist (“ The Heiress of Hyde Park, or the boiled
Flunkey”). She wore a skirt-costume of bright
red, lavishly trimmed with snowy white, and rode
like a bush belle who could ride. The flunk a
nigger dressed in dark green with rosetted
topper and Wellingtons, and a flaring yellow belt,
looked like a gangrened black-pudding with a
pain in its middle.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of OOOtt.,
and eontains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand 'Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, T ancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. Ihe
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
Any Victorian Homer looking round for
an epical theme is recommended to Jos. Symes s
“ Secular Society ” :
This troublous association was initiated in’B2, and the
first row occurred in ’BB between Symes and certain of his
council of thirty—over Symes and co.’s anarchial tenden-
cies. The trustees, secretary, and 16 councillors seceded.
Symes’s crowd then elected new members,_ also new
trustees and a new secretary, excommunicating the old
lot and declaring them bogus. Time passed, and Symes
half-built a hall on the land which was bought by the
undivided whole—but the old trustees held the deeds of
the land. More time passed, and a second secession took
place—anarchy again the trouble. New and old seceders
joined forces, and, having gained admission to the hall
built bv Symes, by stratagem barred the other side out.
Symes’s hall-porter had, however, barricaded himself in
his own room, and at 1 a.m. Symes’s party, provided witn
scaling ladders, effected an entrance into this room. The
other side had, however, barricaded the door from the
outside. The barricade was broken down, and the
besiegers attacked the garrison left to hold the fort, lhe
latter were easily driven out from the back premises, but
the main hall was defended by a stream of cold water from
afire-hose. Symes’s pal'ty, however, gallantly breasted
this, and utterly routed the defenders, who fled into the
street, leaving Symes in triumphant possession. At 7
a.m. the commissariat department of the beaten side
turned up with a large basket of provisions and drink-
ables. A disguised voice hailed them from an upper
window, and asked them to send the basket up
by a rope, it being unsafe (so the voice said) to
open a door. This was done, and Symes and party
then appeared at the window and jeered at the
disgusted commissariat and ate and drank with much
smacking of lips. Then the old trustees successfully in-
stituted proceedings against Symes for possession of the
hall, and defendants and their furniture were thrown into
the street by the sheriff. Nominees from both parties
remained to watch the furniture and effects all night.
Early in the morning the sheriff’s officer left the building,
and' Symes and party rushed it. Proceedings were at
once taken to attach Symes for contempt, but unsuccess-
fully. However, fresh proceedings were successfully taken
for possession, and Symes and party had again to retire,
but they then brought an action against the old trustees
for breach of trust. The case lasted seven days; Symes
lost, and the hall was let to the Spiritualists.
Now Vic. Supreme Court is trying, with much
tribulation, to wind the whole thing up, and won-
dering how on earth to get to work, seeing that
the “Secular Society” was neither limited co.,
unlimited co., nor partnership concern. Mean-
while, Joe Symes sits amid his ruins and raves
and weeps. It’s all really very churchy.

with The Wynne and Tregurtha Patent Battery Plates
are made of iron, cast with peculiarly-shaped ribs. They
are fitted in a wooden frame, and take the place of the

Latest alligator perjury from the North
(the term crocodile is unknown in Northern par-
lance). A citizen of Cooktown secured several
alligator eggs, and, intending to keep them as
curios, placed them inside his shirt for safety on
the way home. Feeling a slight irritation after a
while, he opened his shirt and found that the heat
of his body had hatched the eggs, and that three
sprightly young saurians were burrowing for
tucker in his manly bosom. Next!

The Fiddle and the Bridge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.
From a Letter written by Henry Withering,
Engineer in Charge N.E. Division, to Chief
Engineer, Works Department, Calcutta.
* * * * . .
“ Of course, by this time, you have seraa my report to
the Governor-General on the whole matter , and probably
the incident is looked upon at headquarters as merely i W
unprecedented coincidence. I know what the -
think and say—they say the bridge was destroyed by the
Brahmin high-.priest, who literally played it into destiuc
tion with a fiddle. ,
“ Now, that a whirlwind should come down from. Heaven
and destroy only a powerful suspension bridge," h .
no rigid resistance in any direction, is remarkable » >
for we know these circular storms have a path < 1
drop like thunderbolts : but that this should happen just
when a British force was about to cross it, with a moral
certainty of overtaking the fugitive rajah, who 1 ' ■
ing with him Brahminical treasure valued at millions
sterling—l say, that all this should happen at this
precise moment staggers the imagination. Ik J ~
much interested in the wave theory in physics, and in the
recent expositions of the effects of sympathetic vibration.
I, therefore, wish to recall to your mind a peculiarity of
the Indian fiddle-tbat is, that it possesses subsidiary or
sympathetic strings, not touched at all by tne per-
£o‘“you are familiar with the swaying rock in Cashmere,
estimated to weigh eight thousand tons, which rockea
only at a certain musical festival, and you remenihei pio-
bably that since the destruction of the large bell 111 the
temple hard by, the rock became a fixture , though
may tell you that the priests have been hard at work
casting bells ever since. . . . , T
“ Altogether, as it were in spite of my judgment, I
seem forced to believe that the Hindoos have carried the
vibratory thesis to a stage of w'hich we have on y 1
dimmest conception.”
11.
From the Private Papers of Surgeon H.
R. Macdonald, formerly of the Indian
Army.
# * * *
“ The playing of the man with the fiddle began to weary
me. It had been quite pleasant at first—something of a
solace to my imprisonment—but the monotony of it now
troubled me. The music always beat 111 the same measure,
and ever returned in its melody to the same motive-key.
“ Exactly how long the old Brahmin outside had been
playing I could not tell : he certainly was busy when I
first woke in the morning, and now, though the evening
was drawing in fast, he gave no sign of cessation. Oi the
contrary, it seemed that the volume of sound from his
instrument increased very slightly, biH continually. •
“The other priests, in the small Hindoo laboiers hut
where I was confined, swung their bodies unceasingly to
the regular rhythmic metre of the music. ~
“ 1 had fallen to sleep, and woke again more than once
before I perceived a peculiar straining and whistling
sound which also seemed to mark the time. This alone
hardly interested me, but I sat up suddenly enough on
my mat, with a feeling of more than mere curiosity,
when I first noticed that the beams of the moon, stream-
ing through the hole in the wall which served for a
window, were partly obscured and dismayed alternately,
in accordance with the eternal measure of the music.
And then, in addition, I became aware of the noise of a
yreat wind, which roared up to the hut at the darkening
of the moon, and receded again, only to rush up as before.
The same wind stirred in my hair and rustled the gar-
ments of the priests; but they, all indifferent and with
eyes ecstatically seeing nothing, continued then obei-
sances, yet now more violently and hysterically, as
though their task were approaching some culmination.
“ This indifference of the Brahmm-the unusual
noises—the rush of the tempest the now entire blotting-
out of the moon.by some enormous interposition all this,
happening synchronously with the ebb and flow of the
strains of the Bralnnin’s fiddle, impressed me with I know
U °“ i' pulled out my watch to try and note the rapidly-
shortening duration of the musical period. The wind
had increased to a screaming typhoon, amidst clankmgs
of the chains of ten thousand convicts. I was sensible of
a tremor of the earth. . . Urged by an uncontrollable
terror, I sprang for the door, deflecting with my pinioned
hands, in passing, the ready dagger of one of the priests.
It went ripping through my clothes, but I dashed on and
gained the open. , .
“The Brahmin was Still playing, but no sound from his
instrument was audible ; ttie roar of the huge suspension-
brido-e as it hurtled through the air annihilated all others.
“The waters of the river which the bridge spanned
were forced into a black hollow by the wind which went
with its swing. The clanking noise I had heard was that
of the chains"which supported the bridge, and the darken-
ing of the interior of the hut was caused by the interven-
tion of the bridge in its pendulmn-swing between the
window and the moon.
“ Suddenly I saw the strings of the fiddle fly asunder ,
the restraining chains of the bridge, having passed the
limit of-tension, burst with an awful clang ; and the huge
structure rushed instantly into the river beneath, lhe
hut collapsed, and, in an agony of fear, I threw myself to
the ground , , ,
“ The earth still trembled—the echoes were yet repeat-
ing in the iungle and hids-aad the snow-white waters
were churning hither and thither, as I fled, unpursued,
towards the British lines.’ ’
N.S.W.
J. F. MILLINGTON.
A New York doctor has just successfully
repaired a man who had received 18 pistol-shot
wounds of the' intestines, closing up 12 of them
separately, removing Sin. of gut where four
wounds were too close together, and joining the
separated ends, and closing all 16 holes in the
abdominal wall. Yet the operation did not take
place till 28£ hours after the injury.
Related in the Burnett district that two
travellers came across an aboriginal with his
trousers tusked up making a personal search, lie
succeeded. One of the strangers accosted him.
“ Catch ’em flea ? ” The nig. drew himself up to
his full height, and indignantly replied Me no
blanky dog—catch ’em ’’—well, it was a
word of over four letters.
Well-dressed man enters fashionable res-
taurant ; dines sumptuously and drinks appro-
priate fizz. After coffee, waiter (loq.) : Any-
thing else, sir ? ” “ Yes,” said the guest— bring
me a policeman.” Policeman arrives. -Now,
continues the guest, “ fetch the proprietor, truest
to proprietor : 66 I have had a splendid dinner, but
have no money to pay. Will you please give me
in charge?” Given in charge, accordingly. Guest,
walking down street with bobby : “I sent for
you, old man, so that they should not assault me.
Had you not been there they would have kicked
as weil as imprisoned me.” Lawnorder for ever !
Nine hundred head of cattle, alarmed by
kangaroo-rats, stampeded, the other day, at
Mudgee (N.S.W.), and some of them were not re-
covered. Not long ago a colonial-experience man,
smoking on watch alongside a camp-fire, was sur-
prised to find a steer sniffing at the fire-stick with
which he had just lit his pipe. Out of mischief he
just touched the animal’s nose with the lighted
end, whereupon the beast turned tail and stam-
peded, the whole mob following. Once Billings
menagerie halted at a cattle-station, and in the
early morning an attendant flung open the doors
of the tigers’cage and exposed them —behind the
bars, of course—to the view of the mob of cattle.
At one roar from the tigers the whole mob stam-
peded. In the Crimea, all the horses of a cavalry
regiment stampeded, and were galloping towards
the Russian lines when a bugler had presence of
mind to sound the dinner-call. They all turned
and trotted back. They were British horses.
A recent Melb. insolvency-case discovered
to Victorians the champion useful woman. Prior
to marrying insolvent (who had been living apart
from his wife, paying alimony), witness swore that
she had been manager of a coffee-palace, ana,
pitying 1 insolvent’s loneliness, shared hearth ana
home with him in her cottage. When he was out
of work and his wife was pressing for overdue
alimony, she paid it off and kept on paying it out
of a boarding-house speculation. She also was a
successful dog-breeder, and always commanded
highest prices in this line ; also, to furthei occupy
her time, indulged in poultry -farming, which
brought more grist to the mill. Then, finding she
had an hour or two to spare, she made up pinafores
and children’s dresses, and further increased her
banking-account. Time still hung heavy on he
hands, so she added property-speculation to her
occupations, and worked off several paying house
and land deals. The demon of idleness still pur-
sued her, so the balance of her time was devoted
to money-lending and anything else that came in
her way. Then insolvent’s wife died, and, having
a few minute? unoccupied, she married him ; and
although her multitudinous occupations demanded
increasing attention, yet she had contrived to be
present at the court as a creditor in her husband s
estate for <£lBs money lent. Then, having fired
all this off in a stream of eloquence unbroken by
the slightest pause, she invited further interroga-
tion as to her business capacity ; but, beyond
muttering in an undertone that this was the genius
Premier Turner should have secured to straighten
up the national railway - muddle, the presiding
Judge said nothing except that the meeting was
there and then closed.
Perhaps the most interesting and best
authenticated cavalry duel on record, also the
fairest “test match” of lance v. sword, was the
charge of the British 7th Hussars on the French
Lancers in the main street.of the village of Hemappe
on the day of Quatre Bras, as detailed by bir
Evelyn Wood in “ Cavalry m the Waterloo Cam-
The centre paved roadway has here a wide, umnetalled
track on either side and three vehicles can move abreast
along it without difficulty, It will allow of froml2 to 14
horsemen moving in a line on it. As the head of the
French column of Lancers in red jackets appeared at the
exit from the street the 7th Hussars, working unde the
immediate eye of Lord Uxbridge, were iet; go,and they
galloped boldly at their foe drawn up on t ero ul. 'rhe
Lancers pulling the flank men close to the walls of the
houses, dropped their lance-points and at the halt awaited
the attack. This, was vigorously pushed home, but the
Hussars were unable to penetrate the line of steel
pofnte Both the French and English squadron
leaders hemmed in by the crossed weapons, weie
killed ’ and the Hussars failed to break through
the line of Lancers. They fought desperately for
some time, but were eventually driven back, The tfuceis
then followed them up hut were attacked by a fresh
squadron of Hussars and driven back mto the town.
Again and again were these charges renewed , as Lord
Uxbridge wrote : “ A determined see-saw being kept up
for a considerable time.” Lord Uxbridge eventually
withdrew the 7th Hussars, and riding to i t to
regiment (another light cavalry regiment) ordered it to
advance upon the French Lancers. Our men had, how-
ever, learnt to appreciate the power of the lance, and
Lord U.’s address, “ not being received with all the en-
thusiasm he had anticipated,” he ordered the regiment to
the rear. .
Sir Evelyn Wood, further on in the same article,
states that “ the French lancers were small men
on light horses, and, being new levies, could have
had but a very elementary training. ¥et under
absolutely fair conditions they were able to hunt
off the field one of the crack British Hussar
regiments—which seems strong testimony to the
sujjeriority of lance over sword in cavalry charges.
Thy Gate.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How blest the wandered bird that sings
With such a woodland ecstacy.
Till song is sorrow’s self, and he
Folds on thy roof his fretted wings,
All pain forgotten when with thee I
Thus would my wandered heart achieve,
So far outborne on wayward tide,
A still roof in thy heart, to hide
In utterance with thy soul, and weave
Youth’s dream again, and there abide .
Maoriland.
HUBERT CHURCH.
X Z. • The colour question. In a Melb.
suburb to-day, I met. two “travellers, ’ one white,
the other black. The white man carried the com-
mon s waff. Later on, I met the.same pair in a
pub. The white man still carried the swag. Iho

The defence of trusts, monopolies, and
giant corporations is becoming a fine art in Anaerica.
The latest development is a school
witnesses in the art of giving evidence in court.
This N.Y. seminary is largely attended by peopl
in whose evidence railway co’s, &c., are deep y in-
terested in actions for damages for negligence.
The “ green ones ” are made accustomed to court
procedure, are taught how to treat the jury wit
tact and the Judge with deference and sub-
jected to severe cross-examination by the leading
company lawyers in order to familiarise them with
the real ordeal.
Extract from a teetotaller’s letter on the
Vic. wine-industry, in Melb. Age
Scores of families have been absolutely mmed inthe
north-eastern district by wine-making. Vanou t of
that district, which takes in Eutherglen, have witnessecl
some of the blackest domestic tragedies that ever dis
figured a nation’s history as the result of wme mak g.
One valley alone, extending a distance of about 12
and which presented a scene of flourishing "idustry prior
to the introduction of wine-making, came to■ be sub-
sequently known as “Drunken Gully after the farms
were turned into vineyards. Every once-prosperous home-
stead with the exception of one, was rendered desolate.
I could cite numerous instances of tragic deaths caused
bv the wine, some dying bottle in hand, others found
dead by their own wine-casks, others being killed while
drunk with wine by falling from drays, from horse-
back • and among many other cases I can cite one of
a wife and mother calling upon God to curse the wine,
then taking an axe and staving in every bari'd ni the
cellar. Instances of drunkenness, rum and death abso-
lutely heartrending and appalling, could be multiplied.
If Mr. Buckley is disposed to stigmatise these statements
as exaggerations then let a board of inquiry be appo.mtcd
to make strict, impartial investigation, and it will be
found that the history of wine-making in the north-east
is simply black with horrors. Giving a bonus to such an
« industry ” (?) is very like paying a premium for the luin
of the nation’s manhood and womanhood. The moral
disasters that have occurred in this district m connection
with wine-making are far and «.way beyond the power of
language to exaggerate.
Premier Turner had bettei appoint that ooard of
inquiry at once. The God-forgotten region above-
described should be worth inquiring into.

The First Mate’s Finger.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They had “risen” whales —a perfect
School of them—miles ahead, early in the after-
noon. The Derwent was a “clean ship,” so there
was hurry to be away after the “fish,” even
though it meant a long pull to windward. Two
boats lowered, the first and second mates in charge
respectively. There was little wind, and, but for
the long ocean swell, the sea was calm.
A hard tug at the oars brought them up with
their prey. Both boats were soon “fast” and
travelling swiftly away in contrary directions, in
tow of big whales. The second mate was for-
tunate ; he soon killed his fish, and proceeded to
tow the carcase to the ship, which had worked up
to within three miles of them when they adminis-
tered the coup de grdce. By the time they got to
the vessel it was dark, and, in the excitement of
securing the whale alongside, those on board lost
sight of the other boat, which, when last seen, was
miles away but still “ fast.”
* * * *
The mate had hitched on to a regular
tartar, which, when struck, had started off by
“ sounding ” till nearly all the line was out. Then
he appeared on top again, but before they could
haul up to him he was off once more at racing
speed, dragging them away from the ship. So
the conflict went on. As the afternoon drew to a
close the question of cutting clear was discussed,
but the majority applied the Yankee whalers’
dictum, “ a stove boat or a dead whale,” and
held that the fish could not last much longer, and
that the night being fine, the ship would soon
pick them up. It was nearly dark when they got
up to him and made play with the lances till he
“spouted thick blood.” Then came the “ flurry”
—and a short, mad rush. Ere the crew could pull
clear, so unexpected and sudden was the rally, the
whale came straight at the boat, scattering occu-
pants and gear in the sea. It was his last effort —
the final. A swirl of foam-beaten, blood-tinged
water, and the fish floated dead.
* * * *
Only four of the crew came to the surface—
the other two were not seen again. Probably
stunned, or entangled in the gear, they went to
their last account without a struggle. The sur-
vivors held on the upturned boat and reviewed
matters. Night was closing in, and, strain their
eyes as they might, they could see nothing of the
ship. Then they attempted to right the boat to
ascertain the extent of the damage. This cost
mother life ; one of the hands disappearing sud-
denly. With the fear of sharks in their hearts,
the three who were left clambered on to the boat’s
bottom. Then came the struggle with Death.
The boat, rolling in the trough of the sea, flung
them off as often as they bestrode her, for there
was nothing by which they could retain their hold.
At last one said he had “ had enough,” and, with
a farewell message, gave up, leaving now only the
mate, Johnson, and the boat-steerer.
With words of encouragement to each other,
these two continued the fight. After great diffi-
culty Johnson managed to shove the plug out of
the “garboard strake,” and then was able to keep
his position across the keel of the boat by insert-
ing his right fore-finger—his companion holding
on to him. The blackness of night over and about
them; the eternal swish of water ready to engulf
them as it had engulfed their companions ; no
signs of help. Still, they clung and hoped.
* * » •
All at once Johnson felt his comrade’s
grip slacken, as with a moan the man fell into the
sea. His first impulse was to attempt a rescue,
but he found that he could not now detach him-
self from his place his numbed finger had so
swollen as to completely block the hole. He,
“the last of six,” was left alone. So the night
dragged on.
* * * *
On the Derwent a diligent watch was kept
up through the hours of darkness —the vessel
making short boards to windward the while.
Lights were burnt and guns fired at intervals. At
first signs of daylight eager eyes resultlessly swept
the horizon. It was nearly noon when one of the
look-out-menreportedlargenumbers of birds ahead,
almost in the vessel’s course. Then they sighted
a dark object which was at first mistaken for a
whale. As they got closer they made it out to be
a boat with a figure astride it. It was Johnson,
frantically waving his free arm and yelling to
frighten the birds which every now and then
swooped down at him. A boat’s crew was soon to
his relief, but before they could remove him they
had to chop the plank to release his finger. He
could not have fallen off had he wanted to.
It was a thoroughly unnerved man they received
on board. Now that the worst was over, Johnson
became like a frightened infant, sobbing convul-
sively and talking incoherently, and clutch-
ing and holding on to everything within grasp as
they bore him along the deck. He thought of and
knew nothing beyond holding on for dear life, and
his arms had to be secured ere he could be carried
down the narrow companion-way which led to his
berth.
He pulled round—though he lost his faithful
finger—and fastened to many another whale.
Hobart, Tas.
TAHUNE LINAH.
A Maori Play,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The capture of the Kelly gang in Victoria was an
event which excited the greatest interest among
the Maoriland aborigines. To them Ned Kelly,
the outlaw, was a hero greater by far than the
bravest soldier of whom they had any knowledge.
He was a man whose name they revered, whose
deeds they cherished, whose life was full of merit.
The break-up of the Kelly gang happened at a
time when the Maoris were reaping a rich harvest
from the sale of their lands, and when they had
more money than they knew what to do with.
Some of the leading tribes had gathered into
Waikato to watch their interests before the Land
Court, and all kinds of diversions were indulged in
to make the time pass merrily. There were con-
certs and dances, boxing-matches and drinking-
bouts, horse-races and football-matches.
Some of the native dandies, knowing that I
wrote for the papers, asked me to write a play.
They thought that if I could dramatise the capture
of Ned Kelly they could make it “go” among
their people, and even the Europeans would come
to see it. They grew so enthusiastic over the idea
that they soon overcame any scruples I might
have had, and induced me to undertake a task
for which I felt I was ill-fitted.
J wrote the play. I do not say so boastingly,
for it was not a success. When the curtain went
down on the memorable scene that represented
the final the last act there were no calls of
Author; on the contrary, I honestly believe
that bad the author been recognised in the build-
lng he would have come in for a very bad time
Ihe piece was called “The Last Stand of the
Kellys, or the Battle of Glenrowan.”
There was a stormy time, to begin with, in
settling the cast. Fully half-a-dozen members of
the tribe wanted to take the part of Ned Kelly
and when that was settled there was a prolonged
squabble as to who should be Ban Kelly and Steve
Hart The selection of the lady to take the part
of Kate Kelly gave rise to so much bad feeling
and dissension that for a week the play was at a
standstill. At last the gentleman who was cast
for the part of Ned Kelly, and who was finding
most of the money for the venture, carried his
point and established in the part his sweetheart, a
pum-blooded, excessively-tattooed girl of sixteen.
Ihe play was interpreted into Maori, and re-
hearsals began. The town was billed, leading
people were invited, seats were marked off, and
in every respect the appointments were generous,
this was the bill :
GREAT MAORI PLAY

Capture or Ned Kelly ;
or,
The Battle of Glenrowan.
Maoris and Pakehas. Oh, Listen !
Tins great play will be performed by the famous and
Ngatiraukawa Dramatic Company at the
town Hall on Monday evening.

When the eventful night arrived the curtain
went up on a crowded and demonstrative house.
Ihe great bulk of those present were Maori or
halt-caste. Ihe play was written more as a
tragedy than as a comedy. Yet, as it was inter-
preted, it seemed to be nothing but comedy, or
as the local paper termed it, “a tragedy bur-
lesque. It certainly did suggest the idea of a
screaming farce at some points, and narrowly
escaped, towards the close, resulting in one of the
most real tragedies that the stage has ever wit-
nessed.
The first act was a bush-scene with the sticking-
up of an invisible mail-coach and the rifling of the
passengers, one of whom was knocked on the head
by a bushranger in a manner so realistic that the
luckless fellow fell across the footlights and nar-
rowly escaped being roasted alive among the
fragments of a kerosene-lamp.
The audience roared with delight at this, and
the actors exchanged words of encouragement
with their friends “ in front.”
The second act was a representation of the bush-
rangers’ dance in the Glenrowan shanty, where
the Kellys made merry with the girls before the
police arrived. So as to make the scene thoroughly
realistic, bottles of genuine liquor were put in cir-
culation, with the result that when the curtain
rose on the third and last act there was not a sober
sri^^,„Tkr m TiiS digl,testeffottre “^
•A 11 t} ! e b? an k-cartridges having been exhausted
in the sticking-up scene in the, first act, there were
none available for the battle of Glenrowan, with
which the play was to be brought to a thrilling-
termination. It was therefore agreed that ball-
cartridge should be used, as the audience would
d ‘ fferei \ ce ’ Particularly as all shots
could be fired through the roof and not directly
at the objects of attack on the other side
When Ned Kelly, Dan Kelly, and Steve Hart
appeared, each with a nail-can on his head and
carrying tin armour and a brace of revolvers, there
were shouts of delight. The police-party (some of
them trouserless) came on and retreated, subse-
quently taking up their position at the wings,
10m the shelter of which they fired on the out-
iaws every time the latter emerged from their
iding-place. Ihe shooting, which was slight at
first soon developed into a fusillade, and the
SIVS 18 ! 6 ?! across the stage in all directions
until Ned Kelly was seized round the legs and
carried off triumphantly through the wings, the
wounded outlaw emptying his pistol through the
root as he vanished amidst great cheering.
How act three ended without a fatality I have
never been able to understand. That there were
many narrow escapes was clear from the bullet-
fioles in the w-alls as well as in the roof, some of
those who fired aiming merely over the heads of
them opponents. The youth who took the part of
toteve Hart had a particularly narrow escape—he
got off unwounded, though his visor was pierced
in two places.
